Profit Distribution When Owner Claims Half of All Profit and Mudarib Sub-Contracts
Ruling when owner claims half of total profit and first mudarib gives half capital to second

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on profit distribution when the owner's condition covers the entire profit and the first mudarib sub-contracts half the capital to a second mudarib, explaining why the first mudarib receives nothing.

Core Idea
If the owner claims half of all profit and the mudarib promises half to a sub-mudarib, the first mudarib gets nothing.

This lesson focuses on a specific mudarabah scenario: the owner says 'half of what Allah provides is mine' and the first mudarib gives half the capital to a second mudarib, promising him half of the entire profit. The combined conditions exhaust the profit pool, leaving the first mudarib with zero. We will unpack the reasoning behind this ruling and its analogy to a hiring chain.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and their promises
List the owner, first mudarib, and second mudarib. Note the owner's condition (half of total profit) and the first mudarib's promise to the second (half of total profit).
The owner's phrase 'what Allah provides' is interpreted to mean the entire profit, not just the first mudarib's share.
2
Interpret the scope of the owner's condition
In Hanafi law, the owner's statement 'what Allah provides' or 'what remains' is understood to refer to the entire profit of the mudarabah venture.
This interpretation is crucial; it means the owner gets half of the total profit, not just half of the mudarib's share.
3
Recognize the first mudarib's promise to the second
The first mudarib stipulated for the second mudarib half of the entire profit. This also covers the total profit.
Thus both the owner and the second mudarib have a claim on the entire profit pool.
4
Apply the rule: overlapping claims exhaust the middle party's share
Since the entire profit is claimed by two parties (owner and second mudarib), nothing remains for the first mudarib. He exits without any profit share.
This is analogous to a chain of hire where the first hirer promises the entire wage to the sub-hirer, leaving nothing for himself.
5
Confirm using the qiyas (analogy) to hiring
The text compares the scenario to Zaid hiring Bakr to sew a garment for a dirham, and Bakr hiring another for the same dirham. Bakr ends up with nothing because the entire wage goes to the sub-hirer. The effective cause (illa) is that the same fund is wholly claimed by two parties.
This analogy reinforces the ruling and demonstrates the method of qiyas in Hanafi fiqh.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو قيل : ما رزق الله تعالى فلي نصف أو ما فضل فنصفان ، وقد دَفَعَ بالنصف، فنصفه للمالك ونصفه للثاني، ولا شيء للأول (ولو قيل : ما رزق الله تعالى فلي نصف، أو ما فضل فنصفان ، وقد دَفَعَ بالنصف، فنصفه للمالك ونصفه للثاني، ولا شيء للأول [٣]قوله : ولو قيل : ما رزق الله ... الخ ؛ يعني ولو قال رب المال للمضارب الأول : ما رزق الله فلي نصفه ، أو قال له : ما فضل فبيني وبينك نصفان، والحال أنّ للمضارب الأول دفع رأس المال إلى آخر مضاربة بالنصف، فالنصف لــرب المال والنصف للمضارب الثاني، ولا شيء للمضارب الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ قولَ ربّ المال ما رزق الله أو ما فضل ينصرف إلى جميع الربح ، فيكون له النصف عن الجميع. وقد شرط المضارب الأوّل للمضارب الثاني نصف جميع الربح ، فيكون له أيضاً النصف، فلم يبق للأول شيء، فيخرج بغير شيء، وهذا كما إذا استأجر زيد بكراً ليخيط له ثوباً بدرهم، فاستاجر بكر أجيراً غيره ليخيط ذلك الثوب بدرهم، فلا يكون لبكر شيء من الأجرة.
And if it was said: 'What Allah provides is for me half' or 'What remains is halves', and he gave half [the capital], then half of it is for the owner and half for the second, and nothing for the first. (The matn repeats). [3] His saying: 'And if it was said: What Allah provides...etc.' means: if the owner told the first mudarib: 'What Allah provides is for me half of it', or he told him: 'What remains is halves between me and you', and the situation is that the first mudarib gave the capital to another as mudarabah by half, then half is for the owner and half for the second mudarib, and nothing for the first mudarib. Because the owner's statement 'what Allah provides' or 'what remains' refers to the entire profit, so he gets half of the entire profit. And the first mudarib stipulated for the second mudarib half of the entire profit, so he also gets half. Thus nothing remains for the first, so he exits with nothing. This is like if Zaid hires Bakr to sew a garment for a dirham, and Bakr hires another worker to sew that garment for a dirham; then Bakr gets nothing of the wage.

Commentary
This passage is the second part of page 375, focusing specifically on the scenario where the owner's words 'what Allah provides' or 'what remains' are interpreted as referring to the entire profit, not just the first mudarib's share. The first mudarib gives half the capital to a second mudarib. The owner is entitled to half of the total profit, and the second mudarib is entitled to half (as agreed with the first mudarib). Consequently, the first mudarib receives nothing. The commentary stresses that the owner's condition encompasses the whole profit, and the first mudarib's promise to the second also covers the whole profit, leaving no remainder. The analogy is to a chain of hire: if the first hirer promises the entire wage to the sub-hirer, he gets nothing himself.

Key Terms
جميع الربح (jamīʿ al-ribḥ): The entire profit generated from the mudarabah.
خرج بغير شيء (kharaja bi-ghayri shay'): The first mudarib ends up with no profit share.

Mudarabah Sub-Contracting: Profit Shares When the Mudarib Re-invests
Expands mudarabah rulings on sub-contracting profit shares

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the profit shares of the capital owner, first mudarib, and second mudarib in a sub-contracted mudarabah when given the capital owner's condition and the first mudarib's condition to the second.

Core Idea
The first mudarib's profit share depends on whether the capital owner's condition applies to absolute profit or only to the first mudarib's gain.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains how profit is divided when a mudarib (first) sub-contracts the capital to another mudarib (second). The key variable is the wording of the capital owner's condition: if it covers all profit, the first mudarib may end up with nothing; if it only covers the first mudarib's share, the first still gets a portion. We will work through three scenarios to see how the shares are calculated.

Guided Path
1
Identify the capital owner's condition
Determine whether the capital owner's statement claims a share of the total profit (e.g., 'Whatever Allah provides, half for me') or only a share of the first mudarib's profit (e.g., 'Half of what you earn'). This determines his entitlement from the total.
2
Determine the first mudarib's entitlement from the capital owner
Based on the condition, the first mudarib's own profit share before sub-contracting is either half or a fraction of the total (if the capital owner takes half) or the entire profit minus the capital owner's share.
In the first scenario, the first mudarib gets half of total profit before sub-contracting.
3
Note the first mudarib's condition to the second mudarib
The first mudarib gives the capital to a second mudarib with a condition on the profit share (e.g., half of the profit for the second).
This condition is valid if authorized by the capital owner.
4
Allocate the second mudarib's share from the total profit
The second mudarib takes his agreed share from the total profit, as per the first's condition.
The second's share is taken first from the total, not from the first's share.
5
Compute the residual for the first mudarib
After giving the capital owner his share and the second mudarib his share, whatever remains goes to the first mudarib. If the capital owner's condition was on total profit, the first may receive nothing.
In scenario two, the first gets nothing because the capital owner and second each take half of total.

Source Text

كتاب المضاربة المضارب الذي يضارب ولو قيل: ما ربحت فهو بيننا نصفان ودفع بالنصف، فللثاني نصف، ولهما نصف، ولو قيل : ما رزق الله تعالى فلي نصف أو ما فضل فنصفان ، وقد دَفَعَ بالنصف، فنصفه للمالك ونصفه للثاني، ولا شيء للأول (ولو قيل : ما ربحت فهو بيننا نصفان ، ودفع بالنصف، فللثاني نصف ، ولهما نصف ) ؛ لأن ربح المضارب الأول النصف، وهو مشترك بينه وبين رب المال. (ولو قيل : ما رزق الله تعالى فلي نصف، أو ما فضل فنصفان ، وقد دَفَعَ بالنصف، فنصفه للمالك ونصفه للثاني، ولا شيء للأول قوله : ولو قيل...الخ ؛ يعني ولو قال رب المال للمضارب الأول : ما ربحت من شيء فهو بيننا نصفان ، والحال أن المضارب الأول دفع إلى غيره بالنصف، فلذلك الغير النصف، والباقي بين المضارب الأوّل ورب المال على السوية. قوله : لأن ريح ... الخ ؛ تقريره : إن المضارب الأوّل شرط للمضارب الثاني نصف الربح، وذلك الشرط صحيح ؛ لكونه بإذن المالك، فيستحق المضارب الثاني ذلك النصف على وفق الشرط، والباقي وهو النصف يستوي فيه رب المال والمضارب الأول، فيكون بينهما نصفين، فإنَّ ربّ المالِ لم يشترط لنفسه إلا نصف ما ربحه المضارب الأول، وهو لم يربح إلا النصف. ولا فرق في هذه الصورة والصورة السابقة إلا من حيث اشتراط المضارب الأوّل للمضارب الثاني، فإنَّ في الصورة السابقة شرط له الثلث، فكان ما بقي بينهما، وفي هذه الصورة شرط له النصف، فكان النصف الباقي بينهما. قوله : ولو قيل : ما رزق الله ... الخ ؛ يعني ولو قال رب المال للمضارب الأول : ما رزق الله فلي نصفه ، أو قال له : ما فضل فبيني وبينك نصفان، والحال أنّ للمضارب الأول دفع رأس المال إلى آخر مضاربة بالنصف، فالنصف لرب المال والنصف للمضارب الثاني، ولا شيء للمضارب الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ قولَ ربّ المال ما رزق الله أو ما فضل ينصرف إلى جميع الربح ، فيكون له النصف عن الجميع. وقد شرط المضارب الأوّل للمضارب الثاني نصف جميع الربح ، فيكون له أيضاً النصف، فلم يبق للأول شيء، فيخرج بغير شيء، وهذا كما إذا استأجر زيد بكراً ليخيط له ثوباً بدرهم، فاستاجر بكر أجيراً غيره ليخيط ذلك الثوب بدرهم، فلا يكون لبكر شيء من الأجرة. ولو شرط الأول للثاني ثلثيه فللمالك والثاني شرطهما، وعلى الأَوَّلِ السُّدس ) ؛ لأنَّ للمالك النصف، وللمضارب الثاني ثلثين، فيضمن المضارب الأَوَّلِ السُّدس. (وصح شرطه للمالك ثلثاً والأصل هاهنا : إنّ رب المال متى شرط لنفسه نصف الربح أو ثلثه من مطلق الربح فله ما شرط من جميع الربح ، وإن شرط لنفسه نصف ما ربحه المضارب الأوّل أو ثلثه فله ما شرط مما فضل من نصيب الثاني، ففي هذه الصورة لا يمكن خروج المضارب الأوّل بغير شيء، وفي الصورة الأولى يمكن كما عرفت. قوله : ولو شرط الأول....الخ ؛ يعني ولو شرط المضارب الأول للمضارب الثاني ثلثي الربح ، وقد قال رب المال : لي نصف الربح، فلرب المال النصف، وللمضارب الثاني الثلثان، ويضمن المضاربُ الأوّل للمضارب الثاني سدساً من ماله. قوله : لأن للمالك...الخ ؛ تقريره : إن المالك شرط لنفسه نصف الربح، واستحق المضارب الثاني ثلثي الربح بشرط الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ شرطه صحيح ؛ لكونه معلوما، لكن لا ينفذ في حق المالك، إذ لا يقدر أن يغير شرطه، فيغرم له قدر السدس تكملة للثلثين ؛ لإلتزامه بالعقد، وهذا كمن استأجر رجلاً ليخيط له ثوباً بدرهم، فدفعه الأجير إلى من يخيطه بدرهمين. قوله : وصح شرطه....الخ ؛ يعني لو شرط المضارب لرب المال ثلث الربح ، ولعبدِ ربِّ المال ثلث الربح على أن يعمل ذلك العبد مع المضارب، ولنفسه ثلث الربح، فهو جائز سواء كان على العبدِ دين أو لا ؛ لأنّ اشتراطه للعبد يكون اشتراطاً للمولى، فكأنه شرط للمولى ثلثي الربح. واشتراط عمل العبد غير مفسد ؛ لأنّ العبد أهل أن يضارب في مال مولاه، وللعبد يد حقيقة، ولو كان محجوراً حتى يمنع السيد عن أخذ ما أودعه عبده المحجور، والعبدُ هاهنا صار مأذونا باشتراط العمل عليه، فلا بد لمولاه بعد تسليم المال إليه فصحت المضاربة لزوال يد المولى عن المال.
Chapter of Mudarabah: The Mudarib Who Gives Mudarabah. And if [the capital owner] said: 'Whatever profit you earn, it is between us half and half,' and he gave [capital] for half, then for the second [mudarib] half, and for both of them half. And if he said: 'Whatever Allah provides, for me half,' or 'Whatever remains, it is half and half,' and he gave for half, then half of it is for the capital owner and half for the second, and nothing for the first. (And if he said: 'Whatever profit you earn, it is between us half and half,' and he gave for half, then for the second half, and for both of them half) because the profit of the first mudarib is half, and it is shared between him and the capital owner. (And if he said: 'Whatever Allah provides, for me half,' or 'Whatever remains, it is half and half,' and he gave for half, then half of it is for the capital owner and half for the second, and nothing for the first). His statement: 'And if he said... etc.' means that if the capital owner said to the first mudarib: 'Whatever profit you earn from anything, it is between us half and half,' while the first mudarib gave to someone else for half, then for that other is half, and the remainder is between the first mudarib and the capital owner equally. His statement: 'Because the profit... etc.'; its explanation: The first mudarib conditioned for the second mudarib half the profit, and that condition is valid because it is with the permission of the capital owner, so the second mudarib deserves that half according to the condition, and the remainder which is half is shared equally between the capital owner and the first mudarib, so it is half between them. Indeed the capital owner did not condition for himself except half of what the first mudarib earned, and he (the first mudarib) only earned half. There is no difference between this scenario and the previous scenario except in terms of the first mudarib's condition for the second mudarib, for in the previous scenario he conditioned for him one-third, so what remained was between them, and in this scenario he conditioned for him half, so the remaining half is between them. His statement: 'And if he said: Whatever Allah provides... etc.'; means that if the capital owner said to the first mudarib: 'Whatever Allah provides, for me half of it,' or said to him: 'Whatever remains, it is between me and you half and half,' while the first mudarib gave the capital to another in mudarabah for half, then half is for the capital owner and half for the second mudarib, and nothing for the first mudarib, because the capital owner's statement 'Whatever Allah provides' or 'Whatever remains' applies to all profit, so he gets half of all. And the first mudarib conditioned for the second mudarib half of all profit, so he also gets half, so nothing remains for the first, so he exits with nothing. This is like if Zaid hired Bakr to sew a garment for him for a dirham, then Bakr hired another worker to sew that garment for a dirham, then Bakr gets nothing of the wage. And if the first conditioned for the second two-thirds, then for the capital owner and the second are their conditions, and upon the first is one-sixth. Because for the capital owner half, and for the second mudarib two-thirds, so the first mudarib guarantees one-sixth. (And his condition for the capital owner one-third is valid.) The principle here is: Whenever the capital owner conditions for himself half the profit or one-third from the absolute profit, he gets what he conditioned from all the profit. And if he conditions for himself half of what the first mudarib earns or one-third, then he gets what he conditioned from what remains after the share of the second. So in this scenario it is not possible for the first mudarib to exit with nothing, while in the first scenario it is possible as you have seen. His statement: 'And if the first conditioned... etc.'; means that if the first mudarib conditioned for the second mudarib two-thirds of the profit, and the capital owner said: 'For me half the profit,' then for the capital owner half, and for the second mudarib two-thirds, and the first mudarib guarantees for the second mudarib one-sixth from his own wealth. His statement: 'Because for the capital owner... etc.'; its explanation: The capital owner conditioned for himself half the profit, and the second mudarib deserved two-thirds of the profit by the first's condition, because his condition is valid, being known, but it does not take effect against the capital owner, since he cannot alter his condition, so he (the first) pays him the amount of one-sixth as completion of two-thirds, due to his commitment by the contract. This is like someone who hired a man to sew a garment for him for a dirham, then the worker gave it to another to sew it for two dirhams. His statement: 'And his condition is valid... etc.'; means that if the mudarib conditions for the capital owner one-third of the profit, and for the capital owner's slave one-third of the profit on condition that that slave works with the mudarib, and for himself one-third of the profit, it is permissible, whether the slave has a debt or not, because his (the mudarib's) condition for the slave is a condition for the master, so it is as if he conditioned for the master two-thirds of the profit. And conditioning the work of the slave does not spoil [the contract] because the slave is eligible to engage in mudarabah with his master's wealth, and the slave has actual hand [possession], even if he is restricted until the master prevents taking what his restricted slave entrusted, and here the slave becomes permitted by conditioning work on him, so his master must [allow] after delivery of the wealth to him, thus the mudarabah is valid because the master's hand over the wealth is removed.
Source pages p. 375

Commentary
This passage is a commentary expanding rulings on mudarabah (silent partnership) where the first mudarib (active partner) sub-contracts the capital to a second mudarib. The commentary explains several scenarios based on how the capital owner (rabb al-mal) phrases the profit-sharing condition with the first mudarib. In the first scenario, the capital owner says 'whatever profit you earn, it is between us half and half' (i.e., 50% for each). The first mudarib then gives the capital to a second mudarib with a condition of half profit for the second. The result: the second mudarib gets half of the total profit, and the remaining half is shared equally between the capital owner and the first mudarib. Hence the first mudarib gets one-quarter of the total profit. The reasoning is that the first mudarib's profit is only half (since the capital owner took half), and that half is subject to the condition with the second mudarib, so the first effectively gets half of that half. In the second scenario, the capital owner says 'whatever Allah provides, for me half' or 'whatever remains (after expenses?), it is between us half and half.' Here the capital owner's condition applies to the total profit, not just the first mudarib's share. The first mudarib then gives the capital for half profit to a second. Now the capital owner takes half of total profit, and the second mudarib takes half, leaving nothing for the first mudarib. The commentary compares this to a hiring analogy where a worker sub-contracts for the same wage and gets nothing. The third scenario: the first mudarib conditions for the second mudarib two-thirds of the profit, while the capital owner had said 'for me half of the profit.' Now the capital owner's half (1/2) and the second's two-thirds (2/3) together exceed total profit (1/2+2/3=7/6). The first mudarib must compensate the second mudarib from his own wealth for the shortfall of one-sixth. This is because the first's condition for the second is valid, but it cannot override the capital owner's prior right; the first mudarib is personally liable to fulfill his commitment. Finally, a scenario where the first mudarib conditions one-third for the capital owner, one-third for the capital owner's slave (who works alongside), and one-third for himself. This is valid because the condition for the slave effectively benefits the master (as long as the slave is permitted to work), and the contract remains sound. The commentary emphasizes that the slave's hand is real, and if he is restricted, the master still takes the slave's share. The overall principle: if the capital owner's condition is on absolute profit, he gets his share from total profit; if it is on the first mudarib's profit, he gets it only after the second mudarib's share is satisfied.

Key Terms
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital owner in a mudarabah partnership who provides funds and does not work.
مضارب (mudarib): The active partner in a mudarabah who invests the capital and shares in profit.
ربح (ribh): Profit generated by the mudarabah venture, to be divided as per agreed shares.
شرط (shart): A condition stipulated in the mudarabah contract regarding profit shares.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mudarabah, the capital owner says, 'Whatever profit Allah provides, for me half.' The first mudarib then gives the capital to a second mudarib with a condition of half the profit for the second. What is the first mudarib's share?
A) He gets one-quarter of the profit.
B) He gets one-third of the profit.
C) He gets nothing.
D) He gets half of the profit.

Key Takeaway
The first mudarib's profit share in sub-contracted mudarabah hinges on whether the capital owner's condition is on absolute profit or only on the first mudarib's profit.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
6 min
First Mudarib's Compensation for Overlapping Shares
Ruling on compensation when shares exceed total profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling when a first mudarib stipulates a share for a second mudarib that conflicts with the owner's share, and apply the qiyas (analogy) to a similar case.

Core Idea
A valid condition cannot override the owner's right; compensation ensures the second mudarib's promised share.

In mudarabah, the capital owner and the mudarib(s) agree on profit shares. What happens when the first mudarib promises a second mudarib a share that, together with the owner's share, exceeds the total profit? This lesson explains the Hanafi ruling: the condition is valid, but the first mudarib must personally compensate the shortfall. We will explore the reasoning and the analogous case of subcontracting a tailor.

Guided Path
1
Identify the conflict
The capital owner stipulates half the profit. The first mudarib, before any actual profit, stipulates two-thirds for a second mudarib. The total would be 1/2 + 2/3 = 7/6, exceeding the whole profit.
2
Determine the priority
The owner's stipulation is prior and binding. The first mudarib's condition cannot take effect against the owner's right. Thus the owner gets his half; the second mudarib gets his two-thirds.
The first mudarib's condition is valid between him and the second mudarib.
3
Calculate the shortfall
Since the second mudarib is entitled to two-thirds but only half remains from the owner's share (actually there is a conflict: from the whole profit, owner takes 1/2, second mudarib claims 2/3, leaving -1/6. The second mudarib must receive 2/3 of the total profit, so the first mudarib must compensate the missing 1/6 from his own wealth.
Compensation is 1/6 of the profit amount.
4
Understand the analogy (qiyas)
The ruling is analogous to hiring a tailor for one dirham, who then subcontracts for two dirhams. The original hirer must pay the extra dirham. Similarly, the first mudarib undertook to give two-thirds, so he must make it good.
The 'illa is the undertaking of an obligation that exceeds what can be fulfilled from the original fund.
5
State the final hukm
The first mudarib must guarantee one-sixth (of the profit) to the second mudarib from his own property. The owner's share remains intact.
This is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

(وصح شرطه للمالك تلعاً) قوله : ولو شرط الأول....الخ ؛ يعني ولو شرط المضارب الأول للمضارب الثاني ثلثي الربح ، وقد قال رب المال : لي نصف الريح، فلرب المال النصف، وللمضارب الثاني الثلثان، ويضمن المضاربُ الأوّل للمضارب الثاني سدساً من ماله. (٢) قوله : لأن للمالك...الخ ؛ تقريره : إن المالك شرط لنفسه نصف الربح، واستحق المضارب الثاني ثلثي الربح بشرط الأوّل ؛ لأنَّ شرطه صحيح ؛ لكونه معلوما، لكن لا ينفذ في حق المالك، إذ لا يقدر أن يغير شرطه، فيغرم له قدر السدس تكملة للثلثين ؛ لإلتزامه بالعقد، وهذا كمن استأجر رجلاً ليخيط له ثوباً بدرهم، فدفعه الأجير إلى من يخيطه بدرهمين.
If the first [mudarib] stipulates for the second two-thirds of it, then for the owner and the second is their condition, and upon the first is one-sixth. Because for the owner is half, and for the second mudarib is two-thirds, so the first mudarib guarantees the sixth. And his condition for the owner of one-third is valid. His statement: 'If the first stipulates...' meaning: if the first mudarib stipulates for the second mudarib two-thirds of the profit, and the capital owner said: I have half the profit, then the capital owner has half, the second mudarib has two-thirds, and the first mudarib guarantees for the second mudarib a sixth from his own wealth. His statement: 'Because for the owner...' its explanation: the owner stipulated for himself half the profit, and the second mudarib became entitled to two-thirds of the profit by the first's condition, because his condition is valid since it is known, but it does not take effect with respect to the owner, for he cannot change his condition. So he guarantees the amount of a sixth to complete the two-thirds, due to his commitment by the contract. This is like one who hires a man to sew a garment for a dirham, and the hired worker gives it to someone else to sew for two dirhams.
Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on mudarabah (qirad) when the first mudarib (agent) stipulates a profit share for a second mudarib that conflicts with the capital owner's own stipulation. The author states: if the first mudarib stipulates for the second mudarib two-thirds of the profit, while the capital owner had stipulated half for himself, then the owner gets his half, and the second mudarib gets his two-thirds. However, since the total would be 1/2 + 2/3 = 7/6, which exceeds the whole, the first mudarib must compensate the second mudarib for the shortfall of one-sixth (the amount needed to bring the second's share to two-thirds). The reasoning is that the first mudarib's stipulation is valid as a condition between him and the second mudarib, but it cannot override the capital owner's prior right. Therefore, the first mudarib must make good the deficiency from his own property, analogous to someone who hires a tailor for a dirham and then subcontracts for two dirhams—the original hirer bears the extra cost. This ruling reflects the Hanafi principle that mudarib conditions are binding between the parties but cannot prejudice the capital owner's share.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The agent or manager of capital in a mudarabah partnership; one who invests labor and expertise for a share of profit.
سدس (suds): One-sixth; in this context, the fractional amount the first mudarib must compensate the second mudarib.
ضمان (daman): Guarantee or compensation; the first mudarib's liability to pay the second mudarib from his own wealth.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If the capital owner stipulates half the profit, and the first mudarib stipulates two-thirds for a second mudarib, what must the first mudarib do?
A) The condition is invalid, so the second mudarib gets nothing.
B) The first mudarib gives the second mudarib two-thirds from the owner's share, reducing the owner to one-sixth.
C) The first mudarib must compensate the second mudarib one-sixth from his own wealth.
D) The contract is void because of conflicting shares.

Key Takeaway
The first mudarib's valid condition must be fulfilled personally if it conflicts with the owner's prior share.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate

Stipulating Profit Share for Slave of Capital Owner in Mudarabah
Ruling on profit share for capital owner's slave in mudarabah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on stipulating a profit share for the capital owner's slave in a mudarabah, identify the effect of the slave's debt on entitlement, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding an authorized slave contracting with work stipulated on the master.

Core Idea
The slave's share ultimately returns to the master, unless the slave has debt — then creditors take it.

In a mudarabah (profit-sharing partnership), it is common for the mudarib (worker) to allocate profit shares to multiple parties, including the capital owner's slave who will work alongside the mudarib. This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya clarifies the validity of such conditions and explains who is entitled to the slave's share based on the slave's debt status. It also highlights an important intra-Hanafi disagreement when an authorized slave contracts mudarabah and stipulates work on his master.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Stipulated Conditions
The mudarib stipulates three shares: one-third for the capital owner, one-third for the capital owner's slave (on condition the slave works), and one-third for himself. Determine the legal effect of each condition.
Stipulating a share for the slave is effectively stipulating for the master, as the slave's property belongs to the master.
2
Assess the Validity of the Stipulations
The text declares the mudarabah valid (ja'iz) regardless of the slave's debt. Stipulating the slave's work is not invalidating because the slave has legal capacity to work in mudarabah and becomes authorized by the stipulation.
Contrast this with stipulating work on the capital owner, which invalidates the contract.
3
Determine Entitlement Based on Slave's Debt
If the slave has no debt, his earnings (including the profit share) go to the master irrespective of whether work was stipulated. If the slave has debt and work was stipulated, the earnings go to the slave's creditors. If work was not stipulated, the share goes to the master.
The key distinction is whether the slave is considered a mudarib (if work stipulated) or a stranger (if not).
4
Analyze the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
When an authorized slave (ma'dhun) with debt contracts mudarabah with an outsider and stipulates work on his master, Imam Abu Hanifa holds it valid because the master does not own the slave's earnings due to debt. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad hold it invalid because they consider the master the owner of the slave's earnings even with debt.
This dispute is recorded in al-Hidayah. The mufta bihi (relied-upon position) is not specified here, but later Hanafi works may favor Abu Hanifa's view.

His statement: 'And his condition for the owner...' etc.; meaning: if the mudarib stipulates for the capital owner one-third of the profit, and for the slave of the capital owner one-third of the profit on condition that the slave works with the mudarib, and for himself one-third of the profit, then it is permissible, whether the slave has debt or not, because stipulating for the slave is a stipulation for the master, so it is as if he stipulated for the master two-thirds of the profit. And stipulating the work of the slave is not invalidating, because the slave is capable of mudarabah in the wealth of his master, and the slave has a real hand; even if he is restricted so that the master is prevented from taking what his restricted slave deposits, the slave here becomes authorized by stipulating work upon him, so his master after delivering the wealth to him, the mudarabah is valid because the master's hand is removed from the wealth. This is in 'Al-Bahr' and others. Source pages p. 376

Commentary
This passage expands on the ruling when the mudarib stipulates profit shares for the capital owner and his slave, with the slave working in the mudarabah. The author explains that this arrangement is valid: the mudarib gives one-third to the owner, one-third to the owner's slave, and takes one-third for himself. Since the slave's share ultimately belongs to the owner (as the slave's property belongs to the master), it is as if the owner gets two-thirds. Stipulating the slave's work is permissible because a slave can legally be a mudarib in his master's wealth. The key distinction is that stipulating work on the master himself invalidates the contract, because it prevents the master from delivering the capital. The passage then details the effect of the slave's debts: if the slave has no debt, his earnings go to the master regardless of whether work was stipulated. If the slave has debt, and his work is stipulated, his earnings go to his creditors because he becomes a mudarib with the master's implied permission, and his creditors can claim his earnings. If work is not stipulated, the slave is a stranger, so the share goes to the owner as growth of his property. A separate ruling is given when an authorized slave (who has debt) contracts mudarabah with an outsider and stipulates work on his master: Abu Hanifa permits it because the master doesn't own the slave's earnings (due to debt), so the slave can work. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad reject it because they hold that the master still owns the slave's earnings. The relied-upon position is not explicitly stated but the text mentions the difference. Finally, a note clarifies that if the mudarib's own slave is given a share without stipulating work, it is invalid, and the share goes to the capital owner if the slave has debt, otherwise to the mudarib.

Key Terms
عبد (abd): A male slave owned by the capital owner; in this context, also a potential worker in the mudarabah.
مأذون (ma'dhun): A slave who is authorized by his master to engage in trade; here, the slave becomes authorized by the stipulation of work in the mudarabah.
غرماء (ghurama'): Creditors of the slave; they are entitled to the slave's earnings if his work is stipulated and he has debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the main scenario of the lesson, the mudarib stipulates one-third profit for the capital owner's slave to work. The slave has no debt. Who gets the slave's share of the profit?
A) The slave himself.
B) The capital owner.
C) The mudarib.
D) The slave's creditors.

Key Takeaway
Stipulating a profit share for the capital owner's slave in a mudarabah is valid, and the slave's share belongs to the master unless the slave has debt and his work is stipulated, in which case creditors take it.

Ruling on Mudarabah with a Slave's Labor
Explains mudarabah conditions when a slave is employed

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the conditions under which a mudarabah contract involving a slave is valid, and explain the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding ownership of the indebted slave's earnings.

Core Idea
A mudarabah contract with a slave is valid if the slave's earnings are not owned by the master due to debt.

This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah addresses a nuanced scenario in mudarabah: when a slave is employed in the partnership and his master is also involved. The ruling hinges on whether the slave has debts, because that determines who owns his earnings—his master or his creditors. An intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf between Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn further complicates the matter. Let us walk through the analytical steps to understand the conditions and the preferred position.

Guided Path
1
Identify the parties and the stipulation
Determine who is the capital owner (rabb al-mal), who is the mudarib, and whether work is stipulated on the slave or on the capital owner.
The passage mentions the mudarib giving his slave a share to work with him.
2
Distinguish the effect of stipulating work on the capital owner vs. the slave
Stipulating work on the capital owner invalidates the mudarabah because it prevents delivery (taslim). Stipulating work on the slave does not invalidate because the slave is not the capital owner.
This is a key distinction in Hanafi fiqh.
3
Apply the rule of ownership of slave's earnings
If the slave has no debt, earnings belong to the master. If the slave has debt and his work is stipulated, earnings go to his creditors.
The stipulation of work makes the slave a mudarib in the master's wealth.
4
Analyze the intra-Hanafi dispute when the authorized slave contracts with a third party
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the contract valid if the slave has debt because the master does not own the earnings. Sahibayn hold it invalid because they believe the master owns all earnings regardless.
The issue is the ownership of the indebted slave's earnings.
5
Determine the mufta bihi and practical consequence
According to Al-Hidayah, Imam Abu Hanifa's view is relied upon. If valid, profit goes to creditors; if invalid (per Sahibayn), profit goes to master as growth of his capital.
The thamarah al-khilaf affects distribution.

كتاب المضاربة المضارب الذي يضارب ولعبده ثلثا ليعمل . ولنفسه ثلثاً معه ،[۱] ولعبده ( ثلثاً ليعمل معه ) : أي مع المضارب، ولنفسه ثلثاً. ۳۷۷ بخلاف اشتراط العمل على ربِّ المال، فإنّه يفسد ؛ لأنّ ذلك الاشتراط مانع من التسليم، ثم إن لم يكن على العبد دين فهو للمولى، سواء شرط فيها عمل العبد أو لم يشترط ، وإن كان عليه دين فهو لغرمائه إن شرط عمله، وإن لم يشترط عمله فهو للمولى ؛ لأنه باشتراط عمله صار مضارباً في مال مولاه. وقد صار مأذوناً لما سلف، فيكون كسبه له، فيأخذ غرماؤه وإلا فهو للمولى، وإن لم يشترط عمله فهو أجنبي من العقد، فكان كالمسكوت عنه، فيكون لرب المال ؛ لأنه نماءُ ملكه إذ لا يشترط بيان نصيبه، وإنّما يشترط بيان نصيب المضارب ؛ لكونه كالأجير، وهذا إذا كان العاقد هو المولى. Source pages p. 377

Commentary
This passage from the commentary on Al-Wiqayah discusses the rules of mudarabah when a slave is involved. The author begins by stating a scenario: the mudarib (working partner) gives a share of the profit to his own slave in exchange for his work, while keeping a share for himself. This is contrasted with stipulating work on the capital owner (rabb al-mal), which invalidates the contract because it prevents the capital owner from being excluded from labor. The key issue is whether the slave's earnings belong to his master or to the slave's creditors if he is indebted. If the slave has no debt, any profit from his work goes to his master regardless of whether the contract stipulated his work. If the slave has debt, and his work was stipulated, the profit goes to his creditors because the slave becomes a mudarib in his master's wealth, authorized to earn for his creditors. Without a stipulation of work, the slave is considered a stranger to the contract, so any profit is treated as an increase of the capital owner's property. The passage then presents an intra-Hanafi dispute: if an authorized slave (madhun) contracts mudarabah with a third party and stipulates work on his master, the contract is invalid if the slave has no debt because it is like the master working for himself. But if the slave has debt, Imam Abu Hanifa holds that the contract is valid because the master does not own the slave's earnings (since they go to creditors), so the slave can validly engage in mudarabah. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (the Sahibayn) disagree; they hold that the master does own the slave's earnings, so such a stipulation invalidates the contract. The passage also clarifies that the ruling applies when the contractor is the master, not the mudarib. Finally, it notes that stipulating the slave's work is customary and does not affect the ruling; the profit still goes to the master unless the slave is indebted and his work is stipulated.

Key Terms
عبد مأذون (abd madhun): A slave who has been given permission by his master to engage in trade.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership in profit where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib).
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital provider in a mudarabah contract, who entrusts money to the mudarib for trade.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, what is the ruling when an authorized slave (who is indebted) contracts mudarabah with a third party and stipulates work on his master?
A) The contract is invalid because the master owns the slave's earnings.
B) The contract is valid because the master does not own the slave's earnings due to debt.
C) The contract is valid only if the slave's work is not stipulated.
D) The contract is invalid because it is like hiring the master.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, an indebted authorized slave can validly contract mudarabah with his master's work, because the master's ownership of earnings is suspended by creditors' rights.

Termination of Mudarabah by Death or Apostasy of the Capital Owner
Explains causes of mudarabah termination and effects on agent's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the causes that terminate a mudarabah contract according to the Hanafi school and explain the legal effects on the mudarib's actions after termination.

Core Idea
Mudarabah is a non-binding agency that terminates by death or apostasy of the owner joining Dar al-Harb, by analogy to death.

This lesson examines how a mudarabah (silent partnership) contract ends. Since mudarabah is a non-binding agency, it requires the continuous capacity of the capital owner. The commentator explains that death of either party voids the contract, and that the owner's apostasy followed by joining the Abode of War is treated like death. However, the mudarib's apostasy does not void the contract because he retains legal capacity. Understanding this distinction is crucial for determining the mudarib's authority after the owner's death or apostasy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the nature of mudarabah contract
Recognize that mudarabah is a non-binding (ja'iz) agency contract, which means it depends on the continuous existence and capacity of the principal (rabb al-mal).
This is the foundational principle from which termination rules are derived.
2
List the explicit termination causes
From the text, identify two causes: death of either party (rabb al-mal or mudarib) and the rabb al-mal joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate.
The text also mentions a third scenario: mudarib apostasy does NOT terminate.
3
Understand the proof for termination by death
The commentator states that mudarabah is a form of agency, and agency terminates upon death of the principal. Since the contract is non-binding, it cannot continue after the death of either party.
This is based on the general rule in the Book of Agency.
4
Apply qiyas to apostasy joining Dar al-Harb
The owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate is equated to death (manzilat al-mawt) because his property is divided among heirs and his slaves are freed. The same analogy is applied to terminate the mudarabah.
Before the actual joining, the contract is suspended, not voided, according to Abu Hanifa.
5
Distinguish between owner and mudarib apostasy
If the mudarib apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, the mudarabah does not terminate because the mudarib retains valid speech (legal capacity). The contract is based on the owner's capital and the mudarib's labor, but the mudarib's apostasy does not affect the owner's capacity.
This distinction highlights that the critical element for termination is the owner's capacity and property status.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فصل في العزل والقسمة
وتبطل بموت أحدهما، ولحاق المالك بدار الحرب مرتداً
فصل في العزل والقسمة
وتبطل بموت أحدهما، ولحاق المالك بدار الحرب مرتداً، بخلاف لحاق المضارب بدار الحرب مرتداً، حيث لا يبطل المضاربة؛ لأنَّ له عبارة صحيحة.
[1] قوله: وتبطل...الخ؛ يعني إنّ المضاربة تبطل بموت رب المال أو المضارب، قال: المضاربة وكالة، وهي تبطل بموت الموكل؛ لكونها عقداً جائزاً غير لازم، فكان لبقائها حكم الابتداء، فيشترط قيام الآمر كل ساعة، وكذا يبطل بموت الموكل لقيامها فيه، ولا تورث لكونها غير لازمة وقد مرَّ التفصيلُ في «كتاب الوكالة» فانظر فيه.
[2] قوله: بموت أحدهما؛ سواء عَلِمَ المضارب بموت رب المال أم لم يعلم، حتى لا يملك الشراء بعد ذلك بمال المضاربة، ولا يملك السفر، ويملك بيع ما كان عرضاً لنفس المال؛ لأنه عزل حكمي. كذا في فتاوى قاضي خان.
[3] قوله: ولحاق المالك؛ يعني وتبطل المضاربة بلحاق رب المال بدار الحرب مرتداً؛ لأن اللحوق بدار الحرب بمنزلة الموت؛ ولذا يقسم ماله بين ورثته، ويعتق مدبروه وأمهات أولاده، كما في الموت الحقيقي.
وقبل لحوقه بدار الحرب يتوقف تصرف مضاربه عند الإمام؛ لأنه يتصرف للمالك، فصار كتصرفه بنفسه، ولو تصرّف المالك في هذه الصورة؛ أي إن تصرفه فيه موقوفاً عنده؛ لارتداده فكذا تصرف نائبه.
[4] قوله: مرتداً؛ ولم يعد مسلماً، أما إذا رجع مسلماً جاز جميع ما فعل المضارب من البيع والشراء، وكان عقدهما المضاربة على ما شرطا، أما إذا لم يتصل قضاء القاضي بلحاقه؛ فلأنَّ هذا بمنزلة الغيبة، فلا يوجب العزل ولا بطلان الأهلية. وأما بعد اللحاق وقضاء القاضي به، فالوكيل ينعزل بخروج محل التصرف عن ملك الموكل، وأما هاهنا لا يبطل لمكان حق المضارب، كما لو مات حقيقة. كذا في «الكفاية» نقلاً عن «المبسوط».
[0] قوله: بخلاف لحاق المضارب؛ هذا فائدة قيد اللحاق بالمالك، فالمضارب لو لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً لا تبطل المضاربة؛ لأن له عبارة صحيحة.

And it becomes void by the death of either of them, and by the owner joining the Abode of War as an apostate, unlike the joining of the mudarib to the Abode of War as an apostate, where the mudarabah does not become void; because he has valid speech. So its continuation has the ruling of initiation, so the presence of the principal is required at every moment. Likewise, it becomes void upon the death of the principal because its basis is in him, and it is not inherited because it is non-binding. The detail has already passed in the 'Book of Agency', so refer to it. [2] His saying: 'by the death of either of them'; whether the mudarib knew of the death of the rabb al-mal or did not know, so that he does not have the right to purchase after that with the mudarabah capital, nor does he have the right to travel, and he has the right to sell what was trade goods for the capital itself because it is a constructive dismissal. This is in Fatawa Qadi Khan. [3] His saying: 'and by the owner joining'; meaning that mudarabah becomes void by the rabb al-mal joining the Abode of War as an apostate; because joining the Abode of War is equivalent to death; hence his wealth is divided among his heirs, and his mudabbars and ummahat al-awlad are freed, just as in actual death. Before his joining the Abode of War, the actions of his mudarib are suspended according to Imam Abu Hanifa; because he acts for the owner, so it is like his own action. If the owner himself acted in this situation—i.e., his action is suspended due to his apostasy—then similarly the action of his deputy. [4] His saying: 'as an apostate'; and he did not return to Islam. If he returns as a Muslim, then all that the mudarib did of buying and selling is valid, and their mudarabah contract is as they stipulated. If the judge's ruling of his joining has not yet been passed, then this is like absence, so it does not necessitate dismissal nor invalidation of legal capacity. After joining and the judge's ruling, the agent is dismissed because the object of the action has left the ownership of the principal. But here it does not become void due to the right of the mudarib, just as in actual death. This is in al-Kifayah, quoting from al-Mabsut. [0] His saying: 'unlike the joining of the mudarib'; this is the benefit of restricting 'joining' to the owner. If the mudarib joins the Abode of War as an apostate, the mudarabah does not become void because he has valid speech.
Source pages p. 378

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of Al-Wiqayah, explaining the causes that terminate a mudarabah (qirad) contract. The commentator expands upon the ruling that mudarabah becomes void upon the death of either party (rab al-mal or mudarib) or upon the rab al-mal joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate. The core reasoning is that mudarabah is a non-binding (ja'iz) agency contract, which requires the continuous existence and capacity of the principal. Death naturally ends the contract. Joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate is equated to death (manzilat al-mawt) because the apostate's property is divided among heirs and his slaves are freed. However, before the apostate actually reaches Dar al-Harb, the mudarib's actions are suspended (mawquf) according to Imam Abu Hanifa, since the owner's own actions are suspended. If the apostate returns to Islam before the judge's ruling on his joining, the contract continues. Notably, if the mudarib himself apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, the mudarabah does NOT become void because the mudarib has valid speech (i.e., legal capacity to contract) unlike the owner whose property status changes. The passage also clarifies that the mudarib, upon the owner's death, cannot make new purchases but may sell existing trade goods to recover the capital, as this is considered a constructive dismissal (azl hukmi).

Key Terms
مضاربة (muḍārabah): A partnership where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, with profit shared as agreed.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The owner of the capital in a mudarabah contract.
مضارب (muḍārib): The agent who manages the capital in a mudarabah contract.
عزل حكمي (ʿazl ḥukmī): A constructive dismissal; the mudarib is considered dismissed by the owner's death, but retains limited authority to sell trade goods to return the capital.
لحاق بدار الحرب (līḥāq bi-dār al-ḥarb): Joining the Abode of War (a hostile non-Muslim territory). In this context, it refers to an apostate physically relocating to enemy territory.
مرتد (murtadd): An apostate; a Muslim who renounces Islam.
وكالة (wakālah): Agency; a contract where one person appoints another to act on their behalf.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does mudarabah become void when the rabb al-mal joins Dar al-Harb as an apostate?
A) Because apostasy itself invalidates the contract immediately.
B) Because joining Dar al-Harb is equivalent to death (manzilat al-mawt) and mudarabah is void upon death.
C) Because the mudarib loses his legal capacity.
D) Because the property is no longer owned by the apostate.

Key Takeaway
Mudarabah terminates upon death of either party and upon the capital owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate (by analogy to death), but not upon the agent's apostasy.

When Mudarabah Terminates: Death and Apostasy
Expansion of rulings on mudarabah termination

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the three scenarios of mudarabah termination and explain the Hanafi rationale for each.

Core Idea
Death or apostasy of the investor voids mudarabah, but the mudarib's apostasy does not.

In Hanafi fiqh, mudarabah is a revocable agency contract. Its continuation requires the ongoing capacity of both parties. This lesson examines how death and apostasy affect the contract, distinguishing between the investor and the mudarib.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three termination scenarios
From the text, list the three events discussed: death of either party, investor joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, and mudarib joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate.
Only the first two terminate the contract.
2
Explain the rule for death of either party
Mudarabah is void by death of the investor or the mudarib because it is an agency, which ends with the principal's death. The mudarib's rights do not continue.
This is regardless of knowledge.
3
Analyze the investor's apostasy
The investor joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate is legally equivalent to death: property is divided and contracts terminate. Before joining, the mudarib's actions are suspended.
If the investor returns to Islam before a judge's ruling, the contract remains valid.
4
Differentiate the mudarib's apostasy
The mudarib's apostasy does not void the mudarabah because he has valid speech (ibarah sahihah) and does not own the capital. His transactions are suspended only regarding ownership effects.
The mudarabah continues because the mudarib acts on behalf of the investor.
5
Summarize the legal basis
The rule relies on the nature of mudarabah as an agency, the analogy of apostasy to death, and the distinction that the mudarib does not own the capital.
This is the established Hanafi position from Zahir al-Riwayah.

فصل في العزل والقسمة
وتبطل بموت أحدهما، ولحاق المالك بدار الحرب مرتداً) ، بخلاف لحاقِ المضارب بدار الحرب مرتداً ، حيث لا يبطل المضاربة ؛ لأنَّ له عبارة صحيحةً [١]قوله : وتبطل...الخ ؛ يعني إنّ المضاربة تبطل بموتِ رب المال أو المضارب، قال: المضاربة وكالة ، وهي تبطل بموت الموكل ؛ لكونها عقداً جائزاً غير لازم، فكان لبقائها حكم الابتداء، فيشترط قيام الآمر كل ساعة، وكذا يبطل بموت الموكل لقيامها فيه، ولا تورث لكونها غير لازمة وقد مرَّ التفصيلُ في «كتاب الوكالة» فانظر فيه. [۲] قوله : بموت أحدهما ؛ سواء عَلِمَ المضارب بموتِ رب المال أم لم يعلم ، حتى لا يملك الشراء بعد ذلك بمال المضاربة، ولا يملك السفر، ويملك بيع ما كان عرضاً لنفس المال ؛ لأنه عزل حكمي. كذا في فتاوى قاضي خان».
[٣] قوله : ولحاق المالـك ؛ يعني وتبطل المضاربة بلحاق رب المال بدار الحرب مرتداً ؛ لأن اللحوق بدار الحرب بمنزلة الموت ؛ ولذا يقسم ماله بين ورثته، ويعتقُ مدبروه وأمهات أولاده، كما في الموتِ الحقيقي.

[0] قوله : بخلاف لحاق المضارب ؛ هذا فائدة قيد اللحاق بالمالك، فالمضارب لو لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً لا يبطل المضاربة ؛ لأن له عبارة صحيحة، فإن صحة عبارته لكونه عاقلاً بالغاً، ولأنه صان فيه بعد الردّة ؛ لأنه يتكلم عن عقل وتمييز، كما يتكلم قبل الردّة ؛ ولذا لو أسلم صح إسلامه، وتصرفات المرتد إنما تتوقف عند الإمام ؛ للتوقف في أملاكه، ولا ملك للمضارب في مال المضاربة، فبقيت المضاربة على حالها. [٢] قوله : فـلـو عـلـم.... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ المضارب لـو علـم بـعـزلـه بعـدمـا صـار مـالُ المضاربة عرضاً ، فله بيع عرضها، ولا يمنعُهُ العزلُ من ذلك، فإن حق المضارب قد ثبت في الربح ، وإنما يظهر بالقسمة، وقسمة الربح تتوقف على تميز رأس المال)، وهو لا يحصل إلا بالبيع. (۳) قوله : ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنه ؛ يعني إنَّ المضارب بعدما باع هذا العرض لا يتصرف بالبيع ونحوه في ثمن ما باع ؛ لأن البيع بعد العزل كان للضرورة ؛ ليظهر الربح، ما ولا حاجة إليه بعد النقد، ولا يملك المالك فسخها في الحالة ؛ لأنَّ للمضارب حقا في الربح. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [٤]قوله : ولا في نقد نض... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن مال المضاربة إن كان نقداً من جنس رأس المال حين عَلِمَ المضارب بعزلِهِ لا يتصرّفُ فيه لعدم الحاجة إليه، وهـو معزول.
[0]قوله : أي صار نقداً ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير» : نض الماء ينض من باب ضرب
Section on dismissal and division. It becomes void by the death of one of them, and by the owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, unlike the joining of the mudarib to Dar al-Harb as an apostate, where it does not void the mudarabah, because he has valid speech. [1] His statement: 'and it becomes void...etc.' means that mudarabah becomes void by the death of the owner of the capital or the mudarib. He said: Mudarabah is an agency, and it becomes void by the death of the principal, because it is a revocable, non-binding contract, so its continuation has the ruling of initiation, thus the presence of the commander is required every moment. Likewise, it becomes void by the death of the principal because it is established in him, and it is not inherited because it is non-binding, and the detail has passed in the 'Book of Agency', so refer to it. [2] His statement: 'by the death of one of them'; whether the mudarib knew of the death of the owner or not, such that he does not have the right to purchase thereafter with the mudarabah capital, nor does he have the right to travel, and he has the right to sell what was goods for the capital itself, because it is a constructive dismissal. Thus in Fatawa Qadhi Khan. [3] His statement: 'and by the owner joining'; meaning that mudarabah becomes void by the owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, because joining Dar al-Harb is like death; therefore his wealth is divided among his heirs, and his mudabbars and umm walads are freed, as in actual death. Before his joining Dar al-Harb, the action of his mudarib is suspended according to Imam, because he acts for the owner, so it becomes like his own action. If the owner acted in this situation, i.e., his action is suspended due to his apostasy, so likewise the action of his deputy. [4] His statement: 'as an apostate'; and he did not return to Islam. If he returned as a Muslim, all that the mudarib did of sale and purchase is valid, and their mudarabah contract remains on what they conditioned. If the judge's ruling of his joining did not reach, then this is like absence, so it does not necessitate dismissal nor invalidation of capacity. After joining and the judge's ruling, the agent is dismissed by the removal of the subject of action from the ownership of the principal, but here it does not void due to the right of the mudarib, as if he had actually died. Thus in al-Kifayah, quoting from al-Mabsut. [0] His statement: 'unlike the joining of the mudarib'; this is the benefit of restricting joining to the owner. If the mudarib joined Dar al-Harb as an apostate, the mudarabah does not become void, because he has valid speech: the validity of his speech is because he is a sane adult, and because he acts after apostasy based on intellect and discernment, as he acted before apostasy. Therefore if he becomes Muslim, his Islam is valid. The transactions of an apostate are only suspended according to Imam, due to the suspension of his ownerships, and the mudarib has no ownership in the mudarabah capital, so the mudarabah remains as it was. [1] His statement: 'and he is not dismissed'; i.e., the mudarib, because he is an agent on behalf of the owner, so knowledge of dismissal is required for it, as has passed in the 'Book of Agency'. [2] His statement: 'if he knows...etc.'; meaning that if the mudarib knew of his dismissal when the mudarabah capital had become goods, he has the right to sell those goods, and the dismissal does not prevent him from that, because the right of the mudarib is established in the profit, and it only becomes apparent through division, and division of profit depends on distinguishing the capital, which is not achieved except by sale. (3) His statement: 'then he does not dispose of its price'; meaning that after the mudarib sells these goods, he does not dispose by sale or otherwise of the price of what he sold, because the sale after dismissal was for necessity, to make the profit apparent, and there is no need for it after it becomes cash, and the owner does not have the right to annul it in this situation because the mudarib has a right in the profit. Thus in al-Bahr and others. [4] His statement: 'nor in cash of ready money...etc.'; its summary: If the mudarabah capital was cash of the same type as the capital when the mudarib knew of his dismissal, he does not dispose of it because there is no need for it, and he is dismissed. [0] His statement: 'i.e., it became cash'; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: nadada al-ma'u yaniddu from bab dharaba.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (likely from al-Wiqayah) that states rulings on the termination of mudarabah. It expands on three main scenarios: (1) The mudarabah becomes void by the death of either the investor (rabb al-mal) or the agent (mudarib). This is because mudarabah is a type of agency (wakalah), and agency terminates with the death of the principal. It is a non-binding contract (ja'iz ghayr lazim), so its continuation depends on the ongoing existence of both parties. Therefore, if either dies, the contract is void, regardless of whether the other party knows. (2) If the investor apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, this is treated like actual death for the purposes of mudarabah, because joining enemy territory as an apostate results in the legal separation of property as in death. However, if the apostate returns to Islam before the judge's ruling, the mudarabah is not void. (3) If the mudarib apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, the mudarabah does not become void, because the mudarib retains valid speech (ibarah sahihah) even as an apostate, and he does not own the mudarabah capital. Therefore, his contracts remain valid, though they are suspended (mawquf) regarding ownership effects. Additionally, the passage covers the rule that the mudarib is not dismissed from his role until he actually knows of the dismissal, because he is an agent. If he knows when the capital is in goods (urud), he may sell those goods to realize the profit, since his right to profit can only be determined through sale. After sale, he cannot further dispose of the price, as the need for sale is gone. If the capital is already cash of the same type as the original capital, he has no right to transact at all, as there is no need.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party (rabb al-mal) provides capital and the other (mudarib) provides labor, with profit shared according to agreement.
عزل (azl): Dismissal or termination of the mudarib from the contract by the investor.
لحاق بدار الحرب (lihaq bi-dar al-harb): Joining enemy territory, which for an apostate has legal consequences similar to death in terms of property and contracts.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, why does the mudarabah not terminate when the mudarib apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb?
A) Because the mudarib owns the capital, so his apostasy does not affect it.
B) Because the mudarib has valid speech and does not own the mudarabah capital.
C) Because the mudarabah is a binding contract that cannot be voided.
D) Because the investor must explicitly dismiss the mudarib for apostasy to take effect.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi law, mudarabah termination by death or apostasy hinges on ownership of capital and valid speech, not on apostasy per se.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate

Post-Dismissal Actions of the Mudarib: Rights and Limits
Expansion of post-dismissal mudarib powers

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the rules governing what a dismissed mudarib may do, distinguishing between goods and cash scenarios.

Core Idea
A dismissed mudarib may sell goods to realize profit, but cannot further trade the proceeds or touch cash.

Even after dismissal, the mudarib retains certain rights because of his potential share in profit. This lesson explains the conditions under which a dismissed mudarib can still act, and the limits of those actions.

Guided Path
1
Understand the knowledge condition
The mudarib is not considered dismissed until he actually knows of the dismissal, because an agent cannot be dismissed without knowledge.
This is consistent with the law of agency.
2
Identify the scenario when capital is goods
If the mudarib learns of dismissal when the capital is in the form of goods (urud), he has the right to sell those goods to realize the profit, because profit can only be determined after sale.
This is a limited right for necessity.
3
Apply the limit after sale
After selling the goods, the mudarib cannot dispose of the price (e.g., by buying other goods), because the need for sale is gone. The price is now cash, and he is dismissed.
The owner cannot annul the sale because the mudarib has a right to profit.
4
Apply the rule when capital is cash
If the capital is already cash of the same type as the original capital at the time of knowledge, the mudarib cannot dispose of it at all, because there is no need to realize profit.
In this case, the mudarib is fully dismissed and must return the capital.
5
Summarize the legal logic
The mudarib's post-dismissal actions are permitted only as necessary to preserve his right to profit. Once profit is determinable or not possible, his agency ends completely.
This balances the mudarib's interest with the investor's ownership.

Source Text

[١] قوله : ولا ينعزل ؛ أي المضارب ؛ لأنه وكيل من جهة رب المال، فيشترط فيه العلم بعزله. كما مر في «كتاب الوكالة».
[٢] قوله : فـلـو عـلـم.... الخ ؛ يعني إنَّ المضارب لـو علـم بـعـزلـه بعـدمـا صـار مـالُ المضاربة عرضاً ، فله بيع عرضها، ولا يمنعُهُ العزلُ من ذلك، فإن حق المضارب قد ثبت في الربح ، وإنما يظهر بالقسمة، وقسمة الربح تتوقف على تميز رأس المال)، وهو لا يحصل إلا بالبيع. (۳) قوله : ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنه ؛ يعني إنَّ المضارب بعدما باع هذا العرض لا يتصرف بالبيع ونحوه في ثمن ما باع ؛ لأن البيع بعد العزل كان للضرورة ؛ ليظهر الربح، ما ولا حاجة إليه بعد النقد، ولا يملك المالك فسخها في الحالة ؛ لأنَّ للمضارب حقا في الربح. كذا في «البحر»، وغيره. [٤]قوله : ولا في نقد نض... الخ ؛ حاصله : إن مال المضاربة إن كان نقداً من جنس رأس المال حين عَلِمَ المضارب بعزلِهِ لا يتصرّفُ فيه لعدم الحاجة إليه، وهـو معزول.
[0]قوله : أي صار نقداً ؛ قال في «المصباح المنير» : نض الماء ينض من باب ضرب
Section on dismissal and division. It becomes void by the death of one of them, and by the owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, unlike the joining of the mudarib to Dar al-Harb as an apostate, where it does not void the mudarabah, because he has valid speech. [1] His statement: 'and it becomes void...etc.' means that mudarabah becomes void by the death of the owner of the capital or the mudarib. He said: Mudarabah is an agency, and it becomes void by the death of the principal, because it is a revocable, non-binding contract, so its continuation has the ruling of initiation, thus the presence of the commander is required every moment. Likewise, it becomes void by the death of the principal because it is established in him, and it is not inherited because it is non-binding, and the detail has passed in the 'Book of Agency', so refer to it. [2] His statement: 'by the death of one of them'; whether the mudarib knew of the death of the owner or not, such that he does not have the right to purchase thereafter with the mudarabah capital, nor does he have the right to travel, and he has the right to sell what was goods for the capital itself, because it is a constructive dismissal. Thus in Fatawa Qadhi Khan. [3] His statement: 'and by the owner joining'; meaning that mudarabah becomes void by the owner joining Dar al-Harb as an apostate, because joining Dar al-Harb is like death; therefore his wealth is divided among his heirs, and his mudabbars and umm walads are freed, as in actual death. Before his joining Dar al-Harb, the action of his mudarib is suspended according to Imam, because he acts for the owner, so it becomes like his own action. If the owner acted in this situation, i.e., his action is suspended due to his apostasy, so likewise the action of his deputy. [4] His statement: 'as an apostate'; and he did not return to Islam. If he returned as a Muslim, all that the mudarib did of sale and purchase is valid, and their mudarabah contract remains on what they conditioned. If the judge's ruling of his joining did not reach, then this is like absence, so it does not necessitate dismissal nor invalidation of capacity. After joining and the judge's ruling, the agent is dismissed by the removal of the subject of action from the ownership of the principal, but here it does not void due to the right of the mudarib, as if he had actually died. Thus in al-Kifayah, quoting from al-Mabsut. [0] His statement: 'unlike the joining of the mudarib'; this is the benefit of restricting joining to the owner. If the mudarib joined Dar al-Harb as an apostate, the mudarabah does not become void, because he has valid speech: the validity of his speech is because he is a sane adult, and because he acts after apostasy based on intellect and discernment, as he acted before apostasy. Therefore if he becomes Muslim, his Islam is valid. The transactions of an apostate are only suspended according to Imam, due to the suspension of his ownerships, and the mudarib has no ownership in the mudarabah capital, so the mudarabah remains as it was. [1] His statement: 'and he is not dismissed'; i.e., the mudarib, because he is an agent on behalf of the owner, so knowledge of dismissal is required for it, as has passed in the 'Book of Agency'. [2] His statement: 'if he knows...etc.'; meaning that if the mudarib knew of his dismissal when the mudarabah capital had become goods, he has the right to sell those goods, and the dismissal does not prevent him from that, because the right of the mudarib is established in the profit, and it only becomes apparent through division, and division of profit depends on distinguishing the capital, which is not achieved except by sale. (3) His statement: 'then he does not dispose of its price'; meaning that after the mudarib sells these goods, he does not dispose by sale or otherwise of the price of what he sold, because the sale after dismissal was for necessity, to make the profit apparent, and there is no need for it after it becomes cash, and the owner does not have the right to annul it in this situation because the mudarib has a right in the profit. Thus in al-Bahr and others. [4] His statement: 'nor in cash of ready money...etc.'; its summary: If the mudarabah capital was cash of the same type as the capital when the mudarib knew of his dismissal, he does not dispose of it because there is no need for it, and he is dismissed. [0] His statement: 'i.e., it became cash'; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: nadada al-ma'u yaniddu from bab dharaba.
Source pages p. 379

Commentary
This passage is a commentary on a matn (likely from al-Wiqayah) that states rulings on the termination of mudarabah. It expands on three main scenarios: (1) The mudarabah becomes void by the death of either the investor (rabb al-mal) or the agent (mudarib). This is because mudarabah is a type of agency (wakalah), and agency terminates with the death of the principal. It is a non-binding contract (ja'iz ghayr lazim), so its continuation depends on the ongoing existence of both parties. Therefore, if either dies, the contract is void, regardless of whether the other party knows. (2) If the investor apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, this is treated like actual death for the purposes of mudarabah, because joining enemy territory as an apostate results in the legal separation of property as in death. However, if the apostate returns to Islam before the judge's ruling, the mudarabah is not void. (3) If the mudarib apostatizes and joins Dar al-Harb, the mudarabah does not become void, because the mudarib retains valid speech (ibarah sahihah) even as an apostate, and he does not own the mudarabah capital. Therefore, his contracts remain valid, though they are suspended (mawquf) regarding ownership effects. Additionally, the passage covers the rule that the mudarib is not dismissed from his role until he actually knows of the dismissal, because he is an agent. If he knows when the capital is in goods (urud), he may sell those goods to realize the profit, since his right to profit can only be determined through sale. After sale, he cannot further dispose of the price, as the need for sale is gone. If the capital is already cash of the same type as the original capital, he has no right to transact at all, as there is no need.

Key Terms
عارض (عرض) (arid / urud): Goods or merchandise (non-cash assets) in the mudarabah context.
نض (nadd): Ready cash; money that is liquid.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
After a dismissed mudarib sells the goods (urud) to realize profit, what is he permitted to do with the price (cash)?
A) He may use it to buy new goods if profitable.
B) He may distribute it immediately between himself and the investor.
C) He must not dispose of it further; the sale was for necessity only.
D) He may return it to the investor only after deducting his profit.

Key Takeaway
A dismissed mudarib's authority to sell goods is a limited right to realize profit, not a license to continue trading.

Dismissed Mudarib's Powers: Sale and Exchange by Istihsan
Explains legal limits on a dismissed Mudarib's actions

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the limits of a dismissed mudarib's authority and apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to determine when an exchange of cash is permitted.

Core Idea
A dismissed mudarib may still sell goods and exchange cash of a different type by istihsan to protect his profit right.

This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies the precise limits of a mudarib's actions after dismissal. While agency ends upon knowledge, necessity allows certain steps to realize the profit already earned. Understanding this balance prevents disputes and ensures fair distribution.

Guided Path
1
Identify the trigger for dismissal
The mudarib is not removed until he knows of the removal, similar to an agent. Determine the state of the mudarabah property at the moment of knowledge.
Knowledge is key; without it, all actions remain valid.
2
Determine the asset state at knowledge
The passage distinguishes three scenarios: goods (urud), cash of same type as capital, and cash of different type. Each has a different ruling.
The type of property determines what the mudarib may or may not do.
3
Apply the ruling for goods
If the property is goods, the mudarib may sell them to turn into cash. But after sale, he cannot further dispose of the price (e.g., buy new goods).
The sale is permitted only to realize profit through division.
4
Apply the ruling for cash of same type
If the cash is of the same type as the capital (e.g., both dinars), the mudarib cannot act at all. There is no need for conversion.
No necessity justifies further action.
5
Apply the ruling for cash of different type
If the cash is of a different type (e.g., capital in dinars, cash in dirhams), by istihsan the mudarib may exchange to match the capital, overriding the general analogy (qiyas) that would prohibit any action.
The illa is necessity to realize profit; profit division requires homogeneity.

Source Text
