(ولا ينعزل حتى يعلم بعزله): أي إن عزل رب المال المضارب لا ينعزل حتى يعلم بعزله. (فلو علم فله بيع عرضها، ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنه، ولا في نقد نض من جنس رأس ماله: نص بالصاد المعجمة: أي صار نقداً، ويبدل خلافه به استحساناً). لحق بدار الحرب مرتداً لا يبطل المضاربة؛ لأن له عبارة صحيحة، فإن صحة عبارته لكونه عاقلاً بالغاً، ولأنه صان فيه بعد الردة؛ لأنه يتكلم عن عقل وتمييز، كما يتكلم قبل الردة؛ ولذا لو أسلم صح إسلامه، وتصرفات المرتد إنما تتوقف عند الإمام؛ للتوقف في أملاكه، ولا ملك للمضارب في مال المضاربة، فبقيت المضاربة على حالها. قوله: ولا ينعزل; أي المضارب; لأنه وكيل من جهة رب المال، فيشترط فيه العلم بعزله. كما مر في كتاب الوكالة. قوله: فلو علم... الخ; يعني إن المضارب لو علم بعزله بعدما صار مال المضاربة عرضاً، فله بيع عرضها، ولا يمنعه العزل من ذلك، فإن حق المضارب قد ثبت في الربح، وإنما يظهر بالقسمة، وقسمة الربح تتوقف على تميز رأس المال، وهو لا يحصل إلا بالبيع. قوله: ثم لا يتصرف في ثمنه; يعني إن المضارب بعدما باع هذا العرض لا يتصرف بالبيع ونحوه في ثمن ما باع; لأن البيع بعد العزل كان للضرورة; ليظهر الربح، ولا حاجة إليه بعد النقد، ولا يملك المالك فسخها في الحالة; لأن للمضارب حقا في الربح. كذا في البحر وغيره. قوله: ولا في نقد نض... الخ; حاصله: إن مال المضاربة إن كان نقداً من جنس رأس المال حين علم المضارب بعزله لا يتصرف فيه لعدم الحاجة إليه، وهو معزول. قوله: أي صار نقداً; قال في المصباح المنير: نض الماء ينض من باب ضرب نضيضاً: خرج قليلاً، ونص الثمن: حصل وتعجل، وقال ابن القوطية: نض الشيء: حصل، والناض من المال: ما له مدة بقاء، وقال: أهل الحجاز يسمون الدراهم والدنانير نضاً وناضاً. وقال أبو عبيدة: إنما يسمونه ناضاً إذا تحول عيناً بعد أن كان متاعاً; لأنه يقال: ما نص بيدي منه شيء; أي ما حصل دخل، ما نض من الدين; أي ما تيسر. انتهى. وقال في الصراح: نض: درم دينار نقد شد. انتهى. قوله: أي يبدل نقداً... الخ; يعني إن مال المضاربة إن كان من غير جنس رأس المال بأن كان رأس المال دنانير، والنقد دراهم، أو بالعكس، فله أن يبدل النقد بجنس رأس المال وإن عزل، وهذا استحساناً، والقياس عدم التبديل. وبالجملة; إن المضارب إذا كان معزولاً ومال المضاربة من جنس رأس المال من كل وجه، بأن كان دراهم أو دنانير، لم يتصرف المضارب فيه أصلاً. وإن لم يكن من جنسه من كل وجه بأن كان مال المضاربة عرضاً، ورأس المال أحد النقدين لم يعمل عزله، وتوقف حتى صار مثل رأس المال، وإذا كان من جنسيه من وجه، بأن كان أحدهما دراهم والآخر دنانير، صرفه بما هو من جنس رأس المال دون العروض استحساناً، وإن كان القياس عدم التبديل. قوله: ولا ضرورة; لأن النقدين من جنس واحد من حيث الثمنية، فصار كأن رأس المال قد نص بخلاف العروض، فإنها ليست من جنس رأس المال; لعدم المجانسة بينهما. قوله: وجه الاستحسان... الخ; تقريره: إن الواجب للمضارب أن يرد مثل رأس المال، وهو يتحقق برد جنسه، فكان له تبديله بجنسه ضرورة.
He is not removed until he knows of his removal. So if he knows, he has the right to sell its goods, then he does not dispose of its price, nor of cash that has become liquid of the same type as his capital, and he exchanges what differs from it with it by istihsan. (He is not removed until he knows of his removal): meaning if the owner of the capital removes the mudarib, he is not removed until he knows of his removal. (So if he knows, he has the right to sell its goods, then he does not dispose of its price, nor of cash that has become liquid of the same type as his capital: he specifies with the dotted sad: i.e., it became cash, and he exchanges what differs from it with it by istihsan). If he joins the dar al-harb as an apostate, the mudarabah is not invalidated; because he has a valid statement, for the validity of his statement is due to his being sane and adult, and because he acts in it after apostasy; because he speaks with reason and discernment, just as he spoke before apostasy; hence if he embraces Islam, his Islam is valid, and the actions of the apostate are only suspended with the Imam; due to the suspension of his possessions, and the mudarib has no ownership in the mudarabah property, so the mudarabah remains as it was. His statement: 'And he is not removed' means the mudarib; because he is an agent from the owner of the capital, so knowledge of his removal is required. As passed in the Book of Agency. His statement: 'So if he knows... etc.' means if the mudarib knows of his removal after the mudarabah property has become goods, he has the right to sell its goods, and the removal does not prevent him from that, because the mudarib's right to profit is established, and it only appears through division, and division of profit depends on distinguishing the capital, which is only achieved by sale. His statement: 'Then he does not dispose of its price' means after the mudarib sells this goods, he does not dispose by sale or otherwise of the price of what he sold; because the sale after removal was for necessity; to make the profit appear, and there is no need for it after it becomes cash, and the owner cannot rescind it in this state; because the mudarib has a right to profit. Thus in al-Bahr and others. His statement: 'Nor of cash that has become liquid... etc.' its summary: if the mudarabah property is cash of the same type as the capital at the time the mudarib knows of his removal, he does not dispose of it due to lack of need, and he is removed. His statement: 'i.e., it became cash'; he said in al-Misbah al-Munir: 'nad' water 'yanid' from the pattern 'daraba' nadiidan: came out a little, and 'nadd al-thaman': obtained and hastened, and Ibn al-Qutiyyah said: 'nad' the thing: obtained, and 'al-nad' from property: what has a period of lasting, and he said: the people of Hijaz call dirhams and dinars 'naddan' and 'naaddan'. And Abu Ubaydah said: they only call it 'naaddan' when it turns into specie after having been merchandise; because it is said: 'ma nassa bi-yadi minhu shay' i.e., what income has been obtained, 'ma nadda min al-dayn' i.e., what has become easy. End. And he said in al-Sirah: 'nadd': dirham dinar naqd shud. End. His statement: 'i.e., he exchanges cash... etc.' means if the mudarabah property is of a different type than the capital, such that the capital is dinars and the cash is dirhams, or vice versa, he may exchange the cash for the type of the capital even if removed, and this is istihsan, and by analogy there is no exchange. In summary: if the mudarib is removed and the mudarabah property is of the same type as the capital in every respect, such that it is dirhams or dinars, the mudarib does not dispose of it at all. And if it is not of its type in every respect, such that the mudarabah property is goods and the capital is one of the two currencies, his removal does not take effect, and it is suspended until it becomes like the capital. And if it is of its type in one respect, such that one is dirhams and the other dinars, he converts it to what is of the type of the capital, not the goods, by istihsan, although by analogy there is no exchange. His statement: 'And no necessity' because the two currencies are of the same type in terms of being a price, so it is as if the capital has become cash, unlike goods, for they are not of the type of the capital due to lack of homogeneity between them. His statement: 'The face of istihsan... etc.' its presentation: the duty of the mudarib is to return the like of the capital, and that is realized by returning its type, so he may exchange it for its type out of necessity.
Source pages p. 380

Commentary
This passage is a ruling with istihsan reasoning on the powers of a mudarib after he has been dismissed. The key principle: the mudarib is an agent, so dismissal requires knowledge. Once he knows, his authority is limited. However, to protect his right to profit, he may still sell goods (since profit is only realized by sale and division) but cannot further dispose of the sale proceeds. He also cannot dispose of cash that is of the same type as the capital. But if the cash is of a different type (e.g., capital in dinars but cash in dirhams), he may exchange it by istihsan to match the capital, because the profit is only apparent when the property is of one type and he can return the capital as required. The passage also clarifies that an apostate mudarib does not invalidate the contract, as his statements remain valid and he has no ownership over the mudarabah property. The commentary provides detailed definitions and the istihsan argument: necessity (to realize profit) overrides the general analogy (qiyas) that would prohibit any action after dismissal.

Key Terms
نض (nadd): Cash that has become liquid or specie, i.e., dirhams or dinars after being transformed from goods.
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership contract where one party provides capital and the other provides labor, profits shared as agreed.
عزل (azl): Dismissal or removal of the mudarib by the owner of capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Under what circumstance may a dismissed Mudarib exchange cash of a different type for the capital's type?
A) Always, because he retains full authority until actual removal.
B) Only if the cash is of the same type as the capital.
C) By istihsan, when the cash is of a different type than the capital, to realize profit.
D) He may not exchange anything after dismissal.

Key Takeaway
A dismissed mudarib may sell goods and exchange cash of a different type by istihsan to realize his profit, but cannot dispose of proceeds or same-type cash.

Collection of Debt After Mudarabah Separation
States the ruling on debt collection after mudarabah separation

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the ruling on the Mudarib's obligation to collect debts after dissolution and identify the condition of profit that triggers it.

Core Idea
After mudarabah ends, the mudarib must collect debts only if there is profit; otherwise not.

In this lesson, we examine a concise ruling from the Hanafi school regarding the Mudarib's duty after the mudarabah contract is dissolved. The key question is whether the Mudarib is obligated to pursue outstanding debts owed to the partnership. The text provides a clear conditional answer: the obligation depends on the existence of profit. Understanding this condition is essential for correctly applying the rules of mudarabah termination.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
The act is the Mudarib collecting debts after mudarabah separation. The ruling is wajib (obligatory) conditionally.
The ruling is not absolute; it depends on a condition.
2
Isolate the condition
The condition that triggers the obligation is the existence of profit (ربح). The text says 'إن كان ربح'.
Profit means net profit above the original capital.
3
Note the consequence without profit
If there is no profit ('وإلا لا'), the Mudarib is not obligated to collect debts.
This protects the Mudarib from unnecessary burden when there is no benefit.
4
Consider the context
The separation (افتراق) is by rescission (فسخ) as per the commentary. The ruling applies to voluntary dissolution.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
ولو افترقا وفي المال دين لزمه اقتضاء دينه إن كان ربح، وإلا لا. قوله: ولو افترقا... الخ; يعني إن المضارب ورب المال لو افترقا بالفسخ، وكان
And if they separate and there is a debt in the property, he must collect his debt if there is profit, otherwise not. His statement: 'And if they separate... etc.' means if the mudarib and the owner of the capital separate by rescission, and it was...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This is a brief ruling on the Mudarib's duty after the mudarabah contract is terminated by separation (e.g., both parties agree to dissolve). If there is outstanding debt owed to the mudarabah, the Mudarib is obligated to collect it only if there is profit in the mudarabah. If there is no profit (i.e., the capital is intact or a loss), he is not required to collect the debt. The commentary begins to explain that this applies when they separate by rescission (فسخ), but the text cuts off. The ruling likely protects the Mudarib from unnecessary burden when there is no profit to be realized.

Key Terms
افترقا (iftaraqa): They separated, meaning the mudarib and capital owner dissolved the mudarabah contract.
دين (dayn): Debt owed to the mudarabah property, e.g., from sales on credit.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
When is the Mudarib obligated to collect debts after the mudarabah is dissolved?
A) Always, regardless of profit.
B) Only if there is profit in the mudarabah.
C) Never, because the contract is ended.
D) Only if the capital owner demands it.

Key Takeaway
After mudarabah dissolution, the mudarib's duty to collect debts is conditional on the existence of profit; without profit, there is no obligation.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
6 min
Mudarib's Currency Exchange After Removal: Istihsan Over Qiyas
Ruling with proof: explaining istihsan over qiyas

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi principle of istihsan to determine when a removed mudarib may exchange currency of a different type.

Core Idea
A removed mudarib may exchange currency of a different type because necessity (darura) to determine profit overrides the general prohibition on transactions.

When a mudarib (fund manager) is removed, he generally loses the authority to transact. But what if the mudarabah capital and the cash held are of different currency types? Can he exchange them to unify the type? This seemingly straightforward scenario prompted a classic Hanafi legal analysis weighing qiyas (analogy) against istihsan (juristic preference). The passage before us presents the ruling and its justification, showing how necessity can override the strict analogy.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the Hanafi ruling
The act is the removed mudarib exchanging cash (nadd) when it differs in type from the original capital. The Hanafi ruling is that it is permissible (jawaz) by istihsan, but prohibited by qiyas. This is the mufta bihi position.
The ruling is specific to when cash is a different currency, not goods.
2
State the qiyas position
The obvious analogy (qiyas jali) takes the case of a removed mudarib with identical-type capital, where no transaction is allowed. The illa is removal (azl) plus lack of necessity. Applied here, the mudarib would not be allowed to exchange.
Qiyas is the default; istihsan is an exception.
3
Identify the illa for istihsan
The hidden analogy (qiyas khafi) recognizes that profit cannot be determined unless the capital and cash are of the same type. This necessity (darura) creates a new illa that overrides the apparent qiyas. This is the basis of istihsan.
This is a classic example of istihsan overriding qiyas jali.
4
Distinguish the three scenarios
The passage outlines three cases: (1) capital and cash are identical type → removed mudarib cannot transact at all. (2) capital is goods ('urud) and cash is one currency → removal has no effect; mudarib waits until goods are sold. (3) capital and cash are different currencies → mudarib may convert by istihsan.
Only scenario (3) invokes istihsan.
5
Apply the mufta bihi
In practice, the istihsan position is relied upon. A mufti would permit the exchange, citing necessity. The tarjih authority is from Al-Hidayah (Al-Marghinani).
The ruling is from Zahir al-Riwayah.

[1] قوله : أي يبدل نقدا... الخ ؛ يعني إنّ مال المضاربة إن كان من غير جنس رأس المال بأن كان رأس المالِ دنانير، والنقد دراهم، أو بالعكس، فله أن يبدل النقد بجنس رأس المال وإن عزل، وهذا استحساناً، والقياس عدم التبديل. وبالجملة؛ إنَّ المضارب إذا كان معزولاً ومالُ المضاربة من جنس رأس المال من كل وجه، بأن كان دراهم أو دنانير، لم يتصرّف المضارب فيه أصلاً. وإن لم يكن من جنسه من كل وجه بأن كان مال المضاربة عرضاً، ورأس المال أحد النقدين لم يعمل عزله، وتوقف حتى صار مثل رأس المال، وإذا كان من جنسيه من وجه، بأن كان أحدهما دراهم والآخر دنانير، صرفه بما هو من جنس رأس المال دون العروض استحساناً، وإن كان القياس عدم التبديل. [2] قوله : ولا ضرورة ؛ لأنَّ النقدين من جنس واحد من حيث الثمنية، فصار كأنّ رأس المال قد نصّ بخلاف العروض، فإنّها ليست من جنس رأس المال ؛ لعدم المجانسة بينهما. [3] قوله : وجه الاستحسان...الخ ؛ تقريره : إِنَّ الواجب للمضارب أن يرد مثل رأس المال، وهو يتحقق برد جنسه، فكان له تبديله بجنسه ضرورة.
Meaning: He exchanges ready cash, but it differs from the type of capital: if the capital is dirhams and the ready cash is dinars, or vice versa. In qiyas: he should not exchange it because of the removal and no necessity, unlike goods. The basis of istihsan: profit only becomes apparent when the type is unified, so necessity is realized. [1] His statement: 'i.e. he exchanges cash... etc.' means that if the mudarabah capital is of a different type from the capital, e.g. capital is dinars, cash is dirhams, or vice versa, he may exchange the cash for the type of capital even if removed, this by istihsan, while qiyas is not to exchange. In summary: If the mudarib is removed and the mudarabah capital is of the same type as the capital in every respect, i.e. dirhams or dinars, the mudarib does not transact in it at all. If it is not of the same type in every respect, i.e. the mudarabah capital is goods and the capital is one of the two currencies, his removal has no effect, and it is suspended until it becomes like the capital. If it is of the same type in one respect, i.e. one is dirhams and the other dinars, he converts it to the type of capital, not to goods, by istihsan, although qiyas is not to exchange. [2] His statement: 'and no necessity' because the two currencies are of one type in terms of price, so it is as if the capital has become ready cash, unlike goods, which are not of the same type as the capital due to lack of homogeneity. [3] His statement: 'the basis of istihsan... etc.' Its explanation: The mudarib is obligated to return the like of the capital, and that is realized by returning its type, so he may exchange it for its type out of necessity.
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage is an expansion of a ruling in the matn regarding a mudarib (fund manager) who has been removed (ma'zul) and the mudarabah capital is of a different currency than the original capital. The text first states the ruling by istihsan: the mudarib may exchange the cash (e.g., dinars) for the type of the original capital (e.g., dirhams) even though he has been removed. This is contrary to qiyas (analogy), which would prohibit any transaction by a removed mudarib. The reasoning for istihsan is that profit cannot be determined unless the capital and the cash are of the same type, creating a necessity to exchange. The commentator then elaborates: if the capital is exactly the same type (e.g., both dirhams), the mudarib cannot transact at all after removal. If the capital is goods ('urud) and the original capital is one of the currencies, the removal does not take effect, and the mudarib waits until the goods are sold to match the capital type. If the capital and the cash are of the same type in one respect (both currencies but different metals), the mudarib may convert to the capital's type by istihsan. The proof of istihsan is that the mudarib's obligation is to return the same type of capital, and exchange is necessary to achieve that.

Key Terms
نض (nadd): Ready cash, i.e., liquid currency (dirhams or dinars).
عزل (azl): Removal of the mudarib from the contract, after which he is not to transact.
استحسان (istihsan): Juristic preference that overrides qiyas due to necessity or custom.
قياس (qiyas): Analogical reasoning based on a common cause ('illa).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint

A) Because qiyas also permits it.
B) Because profit cannot be determined until the types are unified, creating necessity.
C) Because the owner consents to the exchange.
D) Because the mudarib is still an agent.

Key Takeaway
Istihsan overrides qiyas to permit a removed mudarib to exchange currency of a different type, ensuring the ability to return the capital type and determine profit.

Debt Collection After Mudarabah Separation: Profit as Wage
Explaining the conditional obligation of the mudarib to collect debts after contract termination.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state when a mudarib is obliged to collect debts after contract separation and explain the legal reasoning that profit constitutes his wage.

Core Idea
Profit is the mudarib's wage; hence collecting debts is obligatory only when profit exists.

This lesson unpacks a key ruling in mudarabah: after the contract ends, does the mudarib have to follow up on outstanding debts? The answer hinges on whether the mudarabah generated profit. If profit exists, the mudarib is like a hired worker who must complete his task; if not, he is a mere volunteer and cannot be compelled. We will trace the reasoning, examine an objection, and see how this principle extends to all agents.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its conditional ruling
The act is the mudarib demanding payment of debts after separation. The ruling is wajib if profit exists, not wajib if no profit.
The key condition is the presence of profit (ribh).
2
Understand the rationale: profit as wage
When profit exists, the mudarib is like an ajir (hired worker). The profit is his compensation, so he must complete the work of collecting debts. If no profit, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot be forced.
This is a qiyas (analogy) to hired labor.
3
Analyze the objection and response
An objector argues: returning capital is obligatory, and that requires possession, so collection must be obligatory. The answer: the mudarib's duty is to remove obstacles (takhliyah), not to physically deliver, similar to a depository (wadi').
The response uses the principle of 'what is necessary for an obligation is obligatory' but distinguishes the type of obligation.
4
Extend the ruling to all agents
The same rule applies to any agent (wakil) who sells goods and then refuses to collect the price. If the agent earned a wage (e.g., profit), he must collect; otherwise, he is a volunteer.
This shows the generality of the principle in Hanafi law.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if they separate and there is a debt in the capital, he must demand payment of his debt if there is profit, otherwise not. (Explanation of 'nadd'): Naddish: came out little, and the price: obtained and expedited. Ibn al-Qutiyyah said: 'Nadd' means the thing is obtained, and 'al-nad' from wealth is that which has lasting duration. The people of Hijaz call dirhams and dinars 'nadd' and 'naddan'. Abu Ubaidah said: They only call it 'nadd' when it becomes cash after being goods, because it is said: 'Nothing has come out in my hand' meaning nothing obtained as income; 'what has become available from the debt' meaning what is easy. End. And in «al-Surah»: nadd: Dirhams and dinars become cash. End. [4] His statement: 'And if they separate... etc.' means that if the mudarib and the owner separate by rescission, and there is a debt owed by people in the capital, the mudarib is obliged to demand the debt legally if there is profit in it, because he takes the profit, so the task of demanding is upon him. If there is no profit, then demanding is not obligatory upon him, because he is a pure agent, and he is a volunteer. [1] His statement: 'he works for wages' because he is like a hired worker, and the profit is like his wages; because he deserves profit by his work, and he has been given the equivalent of his work which is profit, so he is compelled to complete his work, and completing it includes collecting what is due from people as debts. [2] His statement: 'he is a volunteer in the work' and there is no compulsion on a volunteer; do you not see that a donor is not compelled to deliver? If it is said: returning the capital in the manner he received it is obligatory upon him, and that is not complete except by taking possession, and what is necessary for an obligation is itself obligatory, it is answered: what is obligatory upon him is removing obstacles, and that is by vacating (takhliyah) not by actual delivery like the depositary; so if he assigns it to him, his control is removed. [4] His statement: 'for the buyer... etc.' Explanation: The mudarib is the contracting party, and the rights of the contract revert to the contracting party, so it is necessary for him to appoint the owner as agent to demand if he refuses, lest the owner's right be lost, because the buyer pays the price to the mudarib, and he only pays to the one with whom he contracted or to his agent. [4] His statement: 'and similarly all agents' i.e., the agent for sale, if he refuses to demand the price, he is not compelled... because he is like a hired worker, and the profit is like his wages...
Source pages p. 381

Commentary
This passage explains a matn ruling: when the mudarib and the capital owner separate (by rescinding the contract) and there is a debt owed to the mudarabah, the mudarib must collect the debt if there is profit; if there is no profit, he is not obliged. The commentary first clarifies the term 'nadd' (ready cash) with several linguistic authorities. Then it expands the ruling: if there is profit, the mudarib is like a hired worker (ajir) entitled to profit as his wage, so he must complete the work by collecting debts. If there is no profit, he is a volunteer (mutabarri'), and a volunteer cannot be compelled. An objection is raised: returning the capital is obligatory, and that requires possession, so collection should be obligatory. The answer: the mudarib's obligation is to remove obstacles, not to physically deliver; similar to a depository (wadi'). Additionally, if the mudarib refuses to collect, the owner must be appointed as his agent to claim from the buyer, because the buyer's obligation is to the mudarib as contracting party. This rule also applies to all other agents who refuse to collect proceeds.

Key Terms
ربح (ribh): Profit generated from the mudarabah.
وكيل (wakil): Agent; here, the mudarib is an agent for the owner.
مطالبة (mutalaba): Demanding payment of a debt.

Key Terms from Source Passage

B) Because profit is his wage, and he must complete the work to earn it.
C) Because the owner cannot collect debts himself.
D) Because the contract is still valid.

Key Takeaway
Debt collection after mudarabah separation is obligatory for the mudarib only when profit exists, because profit serves as his wage.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Compelling Mudarib and Agents to Collect Debts: Profit vs. Volunteer
Ruling on compulsion of mudarib and agents in debt collection

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to apply the Hanafi ruling on whether a mudarib or agent is compelled to pursue debt collection, distinguishing between profit and volunteer status using qiyas and custom.

Core Idea
A mudarib with profit is like a hired worker—compelled to collect debts; without profit, he is a volunteer and cannot be forced.

In mudarabah and agency, after the contract ends, debts owed by customers remain. Who is responsible for collecting them? The Hanafi school distinguishes based on compensation: if the mudarib has earned profit, he is like an ajir (hired worker) and must complete the work, including debt collection. If there is no profit, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot be compelled. Professional brokers (bayya' and simsar) are always compelled because they work for a fee by custom. This lesson unpacks the legal reasoning (qiyas) and its practical implications.

Guided Path
1
Identify the three categories of agents
Note the mudarib with profit, mudarib without profit, and bayya'/simsar. Each has a different ruling on compulsion to collect debts.
2
Understand the qiyas to ajir
The Hanafi school analogizes the mudarib with profit to a hired worker: both receive a fee (profit = ujrah), so both are compelled to complete the work, including debt collection.
Profit substitutes for fixed wages in mudarabah.
3
Contrast the volunteer status
A mudarib without profit is a pure agent (wakil) who works voluntarily; like a donor, he cannot be forced to complete. He must only delegate the owner.
4
Apply to bayya' and simsar
They work for a fee customarily, so they are in effect hired, and thus compelled to collect debts regardless of profit.
Custom (urf) gives the force of a valid hire.
5
Recognize the legal device (hilah)
Al-'Ayni notes that hiring someone to buy/sell a specific item is invalid; the device is to hire for a day of service then use him in trade.
This ensures the hire is valid.

And the owner is delegated by him (the mudarib), and likewise all other agents. [1] Because if there is profit, he works for a fee, and if there is no profit, he is a volunteer in the work. And the owner is delegated by him: meaning if there is no profit, then the mudarib after separation delegates the owner to demand payment, for the buyer does not hand over the price to the owner of the capital because rights refer to the agent, so the mudarib must delegate the owner. And likewise all other agents: meaning if all other agents refuse to demand payment, they delegate the owners. When there is a debt owed by people, the mudarib is obligated to demand the debt legally if there is profit in it, because he takes the profit, so upon him is the work of demand. And if there is no profit in it, then demand is not obligatory on him because he is a pure agent and is a volunteer. [1] His saying: so he works for a fee because he is like a hired worker, and the profit is like his fee because he deserves the profit by his work, and he has been given the substitute for his work which is the profit, so he is compelled to complete his work, and from its completion is the collection of what is obligatory from debts on people. [2] His saying: so he is a volunteer in the work, and there is no compulsion on the volunteer. Do you not see that the donor is not compelled to deliver? If it is said: returning the capital in the manner he received it is obligatory upon him, and that is not completed except by taking possession, and what is necessary for the obligation is itself obligatory. The answer is: what is obligatory upon him is to remove obstacles, and that is by relinquishment not by actual delivery like the depositary. So when he transfers it, his hand has left it. [3] His saying: for the buyer... etc. Its explanation: the mudarib is the contractor, and the rights of the contract revert to the contractor, so he must delegate the owner in demand if he refuses, so that the right of the capital owner is not lost, since the buyer hands over the price to the owner of the capital, but rather he hands it over to the one with whom he contracted or to his agent. [4] His saying: and likewise all other agents: the agent for sale, if he refuses to demand the price, he is not compelled to demand, but rather he is compelled to delegate the owner to demand the price. [1] Because he is like a hired worker, and the profit is like his fee... (see references). [2] See reference. [3] See reference. The book of mudarabah: the mudarib who conducts mudarabah. And the bayya' and the simsar are compelled to do it. 383 And the bayya' and the simsar are compelled to do it. By bayya' is meant the dallal, for he works for a fee, and the simsar is the one to whom wheat and the like are brought for sale, so he also works for a fee, so both are compelled to demand the price. They are not compelled to demand, rather they are compelled to delegate the owner to demand the price. [1] His saying: both are compelled to do it: i.e. to demand and seek the price, because they work by his order customarily, so that is in the position of a valid hire by custom, so both are obligated to demand and collect. [2] Allamah al-'Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz: If he hires him for a known fee to buy or sell something known, the hire is not valid because he is hired for work he cannot perform himself. The legal device for its validity is that he hires him for a day for service, then employs him in buying and selling until the end of the period. End. Similarly in al-Tabyin and others. [2] His saying: what is meant... etc. Know that bayya' and simsar have been defined by scattered expressions and miscellaneous references. Al-Birjandi said: al-bayya' is the dallal who works for a fee. End. And in Muntaha al-Arab: al-bayya' is a seller or broker who buys and sells. End. All of this agrees with what the commentator mentioned. In Majma' al-Anhur: al-bayya' is one who sells people's goods for a fee. End. Al-Quhustani said: al-bayya', like dharrab, is one who sells people's goods for a fee as in al-Dhakhirah in the tenth chapter of Wakalah. End. And in al-Durar: al-simsar is the one to whom goods and animals are brought to sell for a fee without being hired. End.
Source pages p. 382

Commentary
This passage from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah explains the obligations of the mudarib (manager) and other agents in collecting debts. The main ruling: after the mudarib separates from the capital owner (rabb al-mal), if he refuses to collect debts, he must delegate the owner to do so. The reason depends on profit: if there is profit in the business, the mudarib is like a hired worker (ajir) because he receives the profit as his fee. Therefore, he is compelled (yujbar) to complete his work, which includes collecting debts. If there is no profit, he is a volunteer (mutabarri') and cannot be compelled—just as a donor is not forced to deliver a gift. An objection is raised: returning the capital is obligatory, and it requires taking possession, so the mudarib must do it. The answer: he only needs to remove obstacles (takhliyah), not physically deliver, like a depositary (mudi'). This analysis applies to all agents (wukala') who refuse to collect: they must delegate the owner. The text then distinguishes the bayya' (dallal, broker) and simsar (commission agent) who work for a fixed fee. They are compelled to collect debts because they are effectively hired (ijarah) by custom. The passage also includes a note from al-'Ayni: hiring someone to buy or sell a specific item for a known fee is invalid because the worker cannot perform the act himself; the legal device (hilah) is to hire him for a day of service and then use him in trade. Definitions of bayya' and simsar from various sources are provided, all confirming they work for a fee.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership where one party provides capital (rabb al-mal) and the other provides labor (mudarib), sharing profit according to agreement.
مضارب (mudarib): The manager who works with the capital; he is entitled to a share of profit and is not liable for losses except by negligence.
بياع (bayya'): A professional seller or broker (dallal) who sells goods for others for a fee.
سمسار (simsar): A commission agent who brings goods (like wheat) to sell for a fee without being formally hired (i.e., works on custom).

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the passage, which of the following is NOT compelled to collect debts?
A) Mudarib with profit
B) Mudarib without profit
C) Bayya'
D) Simsar

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school compels debt collection for profit-earning mudaribs and fee-based brokers, analogizing them to hired workers.

Definition and Legal Ruling on Bayya' and Simsar (Brokers)
Definition of bayya' and simsar with ruling on compelled price collection.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to define the terms bayya' and simsar in Hanafi fiqh and state the ruling that they are compelled to collect the price from the buyer.

Core Idea
Bayya' and simsar are agents working for wages; by custom, they are obligated to collect the price for the owner.

This passage clarifies two key terms used in the Hanafi book of mudarabah: bayya' and simsar. Although often translated simply as 'broker', each has a specific technical meaning. The passage also establishes a crucial legal obligation: these agents are compelled (yujbaran) to demand and collect the price from the buyer. Understanding this clarifies the rights and duties of commercial agents in Hanafi law.

Guided Path
1
Identify the terms being defined
Look for the phrases 'المراد بالبياع' and 'السمسار هو الذي...' to locate the definitions.
The text uses both terms in the context of 'yujbaran' (they are compelled).
2
Extract the definitions
Bayya' is defined as the dallal who works for wages. Simsar is defined as the one to whom goods like wheat are brought to sell, also working for wages. Note from the subsequent quotes that simsar may work without being explicitly hired.
The definitions from various sources are harmonized; bayya', simsar, and dallal are treated synonymously here.
3
State the ruling (hukm)
The text states 'فيجبران على تقاضي الثمن' (they are compelled to collect the price). Understand that this obligation arises from custom ('urf) since they work by the owner's command, akin to a valid hire.
The ruling is enforceable by the Qadi; they cannot refuse to collect.
4
Consider the legal stratagem (hila) from al-Ayni
Al-Ayni notes that hiring someone specifically to buy or sell is invalid because the hired person may not be able to perform personally. The valid stratagem is to hire him for general service and then employ him in buying/selling.
This shows a separate fiqhi point about contract validity and legal stratagems.
5
Synthesize the Hanafi position
The bayya' and simsar are not mere middlemen; they have a duty to collect the price. If they fail, the Qadi can compel them. This duty is based on customary practice (ta'mul) and the nature of their engagement.
The ruling is specific to those who work 'bi-amrihi' (by his command) customarily.

Loss in Mudarabah: Deduction from Profit and the Mudarib's Liability as Trustee
Ruling on loss being deducted from profit first and no liability for mudarib beyond profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on loss in mudarabah, explain why the mudarib is not liable for loss exceeding profit, and distinguish between the positions of Imam Abu Hanifa and the Sahibayn regarding invalid mudarabah.

Core Idea
Profit absorbs loss; beyond profit, the mudarib is an amin and not liable.

This lesson examines a key rule in mudarabah: loss is first deducted from profit, not capital. The mudarib is not liable for any loss that exceeds the profit because he is a trustee (amin). We will also explore the intra-Hanafi disagreement on whether this applies to invalid (fasid) mudarabah.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and ruling
Recognize that the ruling concerns loss in mudarabah: it is first charged to profit, and the mudarib does not guarantee any excess.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is the mudarib's status as an amin (trustee), analogous to wadi'ah (deposit). The text also draws an analogy to zakat deduction.
The dalil is implicit: trust implies no liability absent negligence.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
Profit is subordinate to capital, so loss is attributed to the subordinate first. If loss exceeds profit, the mudarib is not liable because he is not a guarantor.
The reasoning is based on the structure of mudarabah and trusteeship.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
Imam Abu Hanifa holds the mudarib is an amin in both valid and invalid mudarabah. The Sahibayn (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that in invalid mudarabah, the mudarib is a guarantor (damin).
This is an intra-Hanafi ikhtilaf.
5
Evaluate the tarjih
The mufta bihi position is that of Imam Abu Hanifa: the mudarib is an amin in both cases. Later Hanafi works adopt this view.
See the commentary for the tarjih.

And al-Ayni said in Sharh al-Kanz: 'al-Simsar, with a kasra on the sin, is the intermediary between seller and buyer, a Persian word arabicized, plural samasirah: he sells and buys for people for wages without being hired.' End quote. And likewise in al-Tabyin, and similar is in al-Nihaya al-Jazariyyah: 'He is the intermediary between seller and buyer to conclude the sale.' End quote. And Allama al-Shami said in Radd al-Muhtar: 'There is no linguistic difference between simsar and dallal; both are explained in al-Qamus as the intermediary between seller and buyer. The jurists differentiate: the simsar is the one who indicates the location of the goods and their owner, while the dallal is the one who accompanies the goods, as relayed by Sari al-Din from some later scholars.' End quote. And Birjandi transmitted from Tahdhib al-Asma': 'al-Simsar is al-Dallal.' End quote. Quhistani said: 'The author's interpretation of bayya' as dallal is not free from criticism.' So the loss is attributed to the subordinate, as in zakat wealth from the contract initially. If the loss exceeds the profit, for example capital is one thousand and profit one hundred, and loss is one hundred fifty, then the mudarib does not guarantee it, because he is a trustee, so he is not a guarantor, due to the contradiction between them in one thing. [2] His saying: 'the mudarib does not guarantee it' applies whether it is from his action or not, and he is believed regarding its loss even if not known, as is said in wadi'ah (deposit). And whether the mudarabah is valid or invalid, it is a trust according to Imam Abu Hanifa. But according to the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad): if it is invalid, the wealth is guaranteed.
Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage presents a ruling on loss in mudarabah. The matn states that any loss is first deducted from profit (before touching the capital). If the loss exceeds the profit, the mudarib is not liable for the excess because he is an amin (trustee). The commentary expands: loss is attributed to the subordinate (profit) before the principal (capital), analogous to how zakat is deducted from the contract initially. An example is given: capital 1000, profit 100, loss 150; the loss consumes the 100 profit, and the remaining 50 loss is borne by the capital, but the mudarib is not liable for it because the mudarib is not a guarantor of the capital. The commentary further clarifies that this applies whether the loss occurred through the mudarib's action or not, and his claim of loss is accepted (as in wadi'ah). Importantly, it notes an intra-Hanafi difference: according to Imam Abu Hanifa, both valid and invalid mudarabah are trusts (amanah) and the mudarib is not liable. But according to Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani), if the mudarabah is invalid (fasid), the mudarib is a guarantor (damin) of the capital. This difference is based on the principle of whether the contract itself creates amanah or not.

Key Terms
أمين (amin): In this text, it means a trustee who is not liable for loss of property in his possession unless he is negligent.
مضاربة فاسدة (mudarabah fasidah): A mudarabah contract that is invalid due to some defect, e.g., unspecified profit share or other condition.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) He must compensate the 50 loss that exceeds the profit.
B) He is not liable for any part of the loss because he is an amin.
C) He is liable only if the loss was due to his negligence.
D) He must cover the entire loss.

Key Takeaway
In mudarabah, loss is first charged to profit; the mudarib is not liable for loss beyond the profit because he is a trustee (amin).

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

8 min
Loss Is First Applied to Profit: The Mudarib's Immunity as an Amin
Commentary on the rule that loss is first applied to profit

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the rule on loss allocation in mudarabah and explain why the mudarib is not liable for losses exceeding profit, citing the principle of amanah and the analogy to wadi'a.

Core Idea
Loss in mudarabah is charged to profit first; the mudarib is an amin, not a guarantor.

In mudarabah partnerships, any loss is first deducted from the profit earned, not from the principal capital. Only if the loss exceeds the profit does it touch the capital, but the mudarib (working partner) is not liable for that excess because he is a trustee (amin), not a guarantor. This lesson unpacks the rule and its rationale from the Hanafi perspective, drawing on the commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'aya.

Guided Path
1
Identify the ruling on loss allocation
The passage states: what is lost (from mudarabah property) is first attributed to profit, not to the capital. This is the core rule.
The loss hits the profit first because profit is subordinate (tabi') to the capital (asl).
2
Understand the rationale – subordinate vs. principal
The commentator explains that profit is like a branch attached to the capital. So any loss is first applied to the branch before the root.
Analogy to zakat: loss in zakat wealth is first applied to the contract before the principal.
3
Examine the mudarib's status – amin vs. damin
If the loss exceeds the profit, the mudarib does not guarantee the excess because he is a trustee (amin), not a guarantor (damin).
Trusteeship (amanah) and liability (damah) are contradictory in one person.
4
Note the intra-Hanafi dispute on invalid mudarabah
According to Imam Abu Hanifa, the mudarib remains an amin even in an invalid (fasid) mudarabah. The two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that in a fasid mudarabah, the mudarib becomes a guarantor of the capital.
The relied-upon position (zahir al-riwayah) is non-liability in all cases.
5
Apply the rule to a concrete example
Consider: capital 1000, profit 100, loss 150. The loss is first set off against profit: 100 absorbed, leaving 50 loss on capital. The mudarib is not liable for that 50.
The mudarib's statement about the loss is accepted (like in wadi'a).

Source pages p. 384

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah. It addresses two interconnected issues: first, the rule that any loss in mudarabah is first charged against the profit, not the principal capital; second, the liability of the mudarib. The commentator explains that the loss is attributed to profit first because profit is subordinate (tabi') to the principal (asl). He gives an example: capital 1000, profit 100, loss 150 — the loss is set off against the profit first (absorbing the 100), leaving 50 loss that touches the capital, but the mudarib is not liable for this because he is an amin (trustee). The commentary then diverges into a lexicographical discussion of the terms 'simsar' and 'dallal' (broker/agent), citing various authorities. This is a typical feature of later Hanafi commentaries (shuruh) where they clarify terminology. Returning to the main ruling, the commentator notes that the mudarib's statement about the loss is accepted (like in wadi'a), and that according to Imam Abu Hanifa the mudarib is an amin in both valid and invalid mudarabah, while the two companions (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad) hold that invalid mudarabah makes the mudarib a guarantor (damin). This is an intra-Hanafi dispute. The relied-upon position (mufta bihi) according to the zahir al-riwayah is that the mudarib is not liable in any case, as the text later states.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership in profit where one party provides capital and the other provides labor; also called qirad.
سمسار (simsar): An intermediary between buyer and seller who facilitates the sale for a fee without being a hired employee.
دلال (dallal): One who accompanies the goods and helps sell them, often synonymous with simsar linguistically but differentiated in fiqh.
أمين (amin): A trustee who is entrusted with property without liability for loss not caused by his negligence or transgression.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a valid mudarabah, if the capital is 1000, profit is 200, and a loss of 300 occurs, what is the mudarib's liability?
A) The mudarib must compensate 100 from his own pocket.
B) The mudarib is not liable for any amount because he is an amin.
C) The mudarib is liable for 300.
D) The profit of 200 is first lost, and the mudarib is not liable for the remaining 100 loss.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's immunity from loss beyond profit is grounded in his status as an amin; only negligence or breach imposes personal liability.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing

Profit Distribution and Subsequent Loss: With and Without Rescission
Commentary on two mudarabah scenarios: profit distribution with and without rescission.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios of profit distribution in mudarabah after a loss and apply the Hanafi rule regarding rescission and profit subordination.

Core Idea
Profit is subordinate to capital: if contract remains active, capital loss voids distributed profit; rescission severs the connection.

In mudarabah, distributing profit before the contract ends can have legal consequences if the capital is later lost. This passage from Sharh al-Wiqayah explains two scenarios: when the contract is rescinded before the loss, and when it is not. The key difference lies in the principle that profit is subsidiary (tabi') to the capital. Understanding this distinction is crucial for correctly handling profit distribution in ongoing ventures.

Guided Path
1
Identify the two scenarios
Read the passage to find the two distinct cases: (1) profit distributed and contract rescinded, then new contract and loss; (2) profit distributed without rescission, then loss.
The commentary explicitly labels these as two cases.
2
Analyze the effect of rescission (faskh)
Rescission terminates the first mudarabah, making the second contract independent. Therefore, loss under the second does not affect the first profit distribution.
This is why the profit is not returned in the first scenario.
3
Apply the principle of profit subordination
The Hanafi maxim 'profit is subordinate to capital' means profit is not secure until the capital is safe. In the absence of rescission, the profit distribution is provisional.
Thus, if the capital is lost, the profit must be returned.
4
Determine the consequences for the mudarib's liability
In both scenarios, the mudarib is a trustee (amin) and does not guarantee the capital shortfall unless there is negligence.
This is reaffirmed at the end of the passage.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
فإن قسم الربح ، وفُسخ عقدها ، ثم عقدت عقداً فَهَلَكَ المالُ كله ، أو بعضه، لم يترادا الربح ، وإن لم يفسخ ، ثم هلك ترادًا ، وأخذ المالكُ مالَهُ وما فضلَ قُسِم ، وما نقص لم يضمنه المضارب [1] قوله : فإن قسم.... الخ ; يعني فإن قسم الربح بين المضارب وربِّ المالِ وفسخ عقد المضاربة، ثم عقدت المضاربة عقد جديد، فهلك المالُ كلّه أو بعضه لم يتراد المضارب والمالك الربح المقسوم ; لأنَّ المضاربة الأولى قد انتهت بالفسخ ، وثبوت الثانية بعقد جديد، فهلاك المالِ في الثانية لا يوجب انتقاض الأولى، فصار كما إذا دفع إليه مالاً آخر. [٢] قوله : وإن لم يفسخ ... الخ ; يعني وإن قسم الربح بين المضارب ورب المال ، والحال أن عقد المضاربة لم يفسخ بأنّ المضارب وربِّ المال اقتسما بعض المال، وتركا بعضه في يد المضارب، على أنه رأس المال، والمقتسم ربح ، ثم هلك المال كله أو بعضه، تراد المضارب والمالكُ الربح المقسوم ، وأخذ المالك رأس ماله ; لأنَّ الربح تابع لما ذكرنا. فلا يسلم بدون سلامة الأصل وما فضل من رأس المال بعد الرد يقسم بين المضارب والمالك ; لكونه ربحاً، فإنَّ رب المال لم يبق لـه حـق بعد استيفاء ماله إلا في الربح، وإن نقص رأس المال بأن لم يكن الربح قدره لم يضمنه المضارب ; لأنه أمين فيه كما مرّ، فلا يكون ضميناً هذا.
If they distributed the profit, rescinded the contract, then entered into a new contract and all or part of the capital was lost, they do not return the profit (to each other). And if they did not rescind, then it (the capital) was lost, they return it (the profit), and the owner takes his capital, and what remains (excess) is divided, and any deficiency (shortfall) the mudarib does not guarantee. [1] His saying: 'If they distributed... etc.' means: if they distributed the profit between the mudarib and the owner, and rescinded the mudarabah contract, then entered into a new mudarabah contract, and all or part of the capital was lost, the mudarib and owner do not return the distributed profit because the first mudarabah ended with rescission, and the second was established by a new contract; loss in the second does not invalidate the first, so it is as if he gave him other capital. [2] His saying: 'And if they did not rescind... etc.' means: if they distributed the profit between the mudarib and the owner while the mudarabah contract was not rescinded, by the mudarib and owner dividing some of the capital, leaving some in the mudarib's hand as capital, and what was taken was considered profit, then all or part of the capital was lost, the mudarib and owner return the distributed profit, and the owner takes his capital because profit is subordinate as we mentioned; it is not secure without the safety of the principal. What remains of the capital after return is divided between the mudarib and owner because it is profit; the owner has no right after recovering his money except in the profit. And if the capital is deficient (i.e., the profit does not cover the loss), the mudarib does not guarantee it because he is a trustee as previously, so he is not a guarantor.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage explains two scenarios regarding profit distribution in mudarabah. First scenario: The mudarib and owner distribute the profit, then they rescind (faskh) the original mudarabah contract and enter into a new one. If after that some or all of the capital is lost, the previously distributed profit is not returned because the first contract is terminated by rescission, and the loss occurs under a new contract. This new contract is independent, so the profit from the first contract stands. The second scenario: The profit is distributed without rescinding the contract (i.e., they partially distribute the capital as profit while the contract remains active). If then the remaining capital is lost, the distributed profit must be returned because the profit is subsidiary to the capital: if the capital is lost, the profit cannot stand alone. The owner takes back his full capital (if possible), and any surplus after that is considered profit and divided. If the loss exceeds the profit, leaving a capital deficiency, the mudarib does not compensate the shortfall because he is an amin (trustee), and his liability is only for negligence or violation, not for normal business losses.

Key Terms
فسخ (faskh): Rescission or annulment of the mudarabah contract, terminating it.
تراد (tarada): Returning (the distributed profit) to each other; mutual redelivery.
ربح (ribh): Profit generated from the mudarabah, which is subordinate to the capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In a mudarabah, if the profit is distributed while the contract is still in effect, and then the remaining capital is lost, what happens?
A) The distributed profit is kept by the parties and the loss is borne by the owner only.
B) The distributed profit must be returned, and any deficiency in capital is not guaranteed by the mudarib.
C) The mudarib must compensate the owner for the lost capital.
D) The profit distribution is void and the contract is automatically rescinded.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi principle: profit is subordinate to capital; rescission severs the connection, protecting distributed profit.

Profit Distribution and Loss in Mudarabah: Dissolution vs. Continuation
Ruling on profit division and contract dissolution with loss

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between the two scenarios of profit division in mudarabah (with and without contract dissolution) and apply the Hanafi ruling on liability for losses.

Core Idea
Divided profit is final only if the contract is dissolved; if ongoing, loss of principal requires returning profit.

This lesson examines two critical scenarios in mudarabah: when profit is divided and the contract is dissolved versus when profit is divided but the contract continues. Understanding the difference is essential for correctly handling losses that occur after profit distribution. We will also explore an intra-Hanafi dispute on the mudarib's liability.

Guided Path
1
Identify the Two Scenarios
Read the passage and list the two distinct cases: (1) profit divided and contract dissolved, then new contract, then loss; (2) profit divided but contract not dissolved, then loss.
The ruling differs based on whether the original contract was dissolved.
2
Locate the Condition (Dissolution)
The key condition is the act of dissolution (faskh) before the loss. Determine how this affects the status of the divided profit.
Dissolution makes the first contract independent from the second.
3
Understand the Rationale
The passage explains that when dissolved, the profit is not returned because the contracts are separate; when not dissolved, profit is subsidiary to principal and must be restored if principal is lost.
This is a rational (aqli) proof: profit follows principal unless the contract is terminated.
4
Note the Intra-Hanafi Dispute
Al-Tahawi differed on the liability question; the majority (zahir al-riwayah) holds no liability in all cases. Identify the practical implication (thamarah al-khilaf).
Under Tahawi, the mudarib might be liable for loss even in the first scenario; the majority says no.

⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

يمي
Book of Mudarabah. The mudarib who conducts mudarabah. If profit is divided, and its contract is dissolved, then a new contract is made, and the entire capital is lost or part of it, they do not return the profit. And if it is not dissolved, then it is lost, they return it. And the owner takes his capital, and whatever remains is divided, and whatever deficiency there is, the mudarib does not guarantee it. (If profit is divided, and its contract is dissolved, then a contract is made and the entire capital is lost or part of it, they do not return the profit): meaning the contract is dissolved while the capital is in the hands of the mudarib, then they contract (again), then the capital is lost. (And if it is not dissolved, then it is lost, they return it, and the owner takes his capital and whatever remains is divided, and whatever deficiency there is, the mudarib does not guarantee it.) Al-Minh mentions it. And it is the saying of al-Tahawi for him, but the apparent transmission is no liability in all cases, and it has preceded. His saying: 'If profit is divided... etc.'; meaning: if profit is divided between the mudarib and the capital owner and the mudarabah contract is dissolved, then the mudarabah contracts anew, and all or part of the capital is lost, the mudarib and the owner do not return the divided profit; because the first mudarabah has ended with dissolution, and the second is established by a new contract, so the loss of capital in the second does not require overturning the first, so it is as if he gave him another capital. His saying: 'And if it is not dissolved... etc.'; meaning: if profit is divided between the mudarib and the capital owner, while the mudarabah contract is not dissolved—that is, the mudarib and the owner divided some of the capital and left some in the mudarib's hand as capital, and what was divided is profit—then all or part of the capital is lost, the mudarib and the owner return the divided profit, and the owner takes his capital; because profit is subsidiary to what we mentioned. So it does not remain safe without the safety of the principal. And whatever remains of the capital after reversion is divided between the mudarib and the owner, being profit; for the owner has no right after receiving his capital except in the profit. And if the capital is deficient—i.e., the profit is not sufficient to cover it—the mudarib does not guarantee it, because he is a trustee as previously, so he is not a guarantor. And al-Allamah al-Aqta' Allah said in Sharh Mukhtasar al-Quduri: 'Our companions said: if the mudarabah capital is lost before purchasing anything with it, the mudarabah becomes void; because the mudarabah is specifically tied to what it was concluded upon, like a deposit. And if it becomes specific, the contract becomes void by its loss. The mudarib's word is accepted regarding its loss because he is a trustee. If the mudarib consumes it, he guarantees it, and he may not thereafter purchase anything on mudarabah because he becomes a guarantor. And it is a rule that the mudarib is a trustee. If someone else consumes it and he takes compensation from him, it is permissible for him to purchase with it on mudarabah, because when he took its substitute, it is as if he took its price.' [End] to the extent of necessity. Yami.
Source pages p. 385

Commentary
This passage is a ruling on mudarabah regarding profit distribution and contract dissolution. The text first states two scenarios: (1) If profit is divided and then the contract is dissolved, and afterwards a new contract is made, and then the capital is lost (in whole or part), the parties do not return the previously divided profit. The reason is that the first mudarabah ended with the dissolution, and the second contract is independent; loss in the second does not affect the first. This is analogous to giving another capital. (2) If profit is divided but the contract is not dissolved (i.e., they only divided some profit and left some capital as principal), and then the capital is lost, the parties must return the divided profit. Because the profit is subsidiary to the principal; if the principal is not safe, the profit must be restored. The owner takes his capital, and any surplus is divided as profit. If the capital is deficient (i.e., the loss exceeds the profit), the mudarib does not guarantee the deficiency because he is a trustee. The commentary then mentions al-Tahawi's opinion that there is no liability in all cases, but the apparent (zahir al-riwayah) ruling is no liability in all cases, as previously mentioned. The passage also quotes al-Aqta' on the case where capital is lost before any purchase: the mudarabah becomes void because it is tied to that specific capital like a deposit. The mudarib's word is accepted regarding loss (as trustee), but if he misappropriates it, he becomes a guarantor. If a third party misappropriates it and the mudarib takes compensation, he can still use it for mudarabah because it substitutes the original capital.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership in which one party provides capital and the other provides labor/management for a share in profits.
فسخ (faskh): Dissolution or rescission of the contract by mutual agreement or operation of law.
ربح (ribh): Profit generated from the mudarabah venture, distributed according to the agreed ratio.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the Hanafi school, what is the ruling when profit is divided and the mudarabah contract is dissolved, then a new contract is made, and the new capital is lost?
A) The divided profit must be returned to the owner.
B) The divided profit is not returned.
C) The mudarib must personally compensate the loss.
D) The contract is void and the profit becomes invalid.

Key Takeaway
The finality of divided profit in mudarabah depends on whether the contract is dissolved before loss occurs.

The Mudarib's Expenses: In His City and During Travel
Expands the ruling on mudarib's expenses from matn of al-Wiqayah

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi rule for which expenses of the mudarib are from the mudarabah capital and which are from his personal wealth, and identify the criteria for determining 'his city' in this context.

Core Idea
Mudarib's personal expenses come from his own wealth when at home; travel expenses are from partnership capital.

A mudarib's living expenses are not always charged to the mudarabah capital. The Hanafi school distinguishes based on location: in his own city, expenses are personal; while traveling for the partnership, they become partnership costs. This lesson unpacks the definition of 'his city' and the exception for medicine, which remains a personal expense even in travel.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and the default ruling
The mudarib's expense (nafaqah) in his city is from his own wealth. The matn states: 'and the expense of a mudarib who works in his city is from his own wealth, like his medicine.'
The ruling applies to a valid mudarabah; in an invalid one, the mudarib is an ajir and not entitled to expenses.
2
Define 'his city' (misr)
The city where he resides, whether original homeland or adopted by marriage. If he resides temporarily for partnership but does not settle, expenses remain personal until he travels and returns for trade.
Al-Mabsut distinguishes: if he returns after travel, expenses become partnership's; if he marries and settles, they revert to personal.
3
Apply the rule to travel
During travel for the mudarabah, all necessary expenses (food, drink, clothing, servant, laundry, oil) are from the capital.
Travel excludes residence in a city taken as a home; even if he intends to stay 15 days but not as a home, expenses are still from capital.
4
Handle the exception for medicine
Medicine is always from the mudarib's own wealth, whether in residence or travel, according to the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya).
This is because need for medicine is not certain, unlike daily sustenance.

فصل فيما يفعله المضارب
[٢]
ونفقة مضارب عمل في مصره في ماله كدوائه)
[١] قوله : ونفقة مضارب ؛ أي مضاربة صحيحة، فإن المضارب في المضاربة الفاسدة لا يستحق النفقة ؛ لكونه أجيرا، كما صرحوا به.
[٢] قوله : في مصره ؛ قال البِرْجَندي في شرح النقاية»: فالمراد بمصره ؛ المصرُ الذي أقام فيه سواء كان وطناً أصلياً أو لا، فلو وقع عقد المضاربة بالكوفة، وهي ليست وطناً أصليّاً ،له فما دام فيها نفقته في ماله، فإن سافر وعاد إلى الكوفة في تجارته كان نفقته في مال المضاربة. كذا في «الخزانة». انتهى.
وقال في «المبسوط»: ولو دفع المال إليه مضاربة، وهما بالكوفة وليست الكوفة بوطن للمضارب لم ينفق على نفسه من المال ما دام في الكوفة ؛ لأنَّ إقامته فيها ليست للمضاربة، فلا يستوجب النفقة ما لم يخرج منها إلى وطنه، ثم عاد إليها في تجارته، أنفق في الكوفة من مال المضاربة ؛ لأنه وطنه بها كان مستعاراً وقد انتقض بالسفر. فرجوعه بعد ذلك إلى الكوفة وذهابه إلى مصر آخر سواء، وإن تزوج امرأة واتخذها وطناً زالت نفقته من مال المضاربة ؛ لأنّ مقامه لها بعدما تزوج كان لأجل أهله بمنزلة وطنه الأصلي، ذكره العلامةُ الطَّحْطَاوِي (۱) نقلاً عن ملكي، وهكذا في «البحر»(۲) نقلا عن «البدائع» و«المحيط» و«الفتاوى الظهيرية».
فما في «الدر المختار» : إذا نوى الإقامة بمصر ولم يتخذه داراً فله النفقةُ ما لم يأخذ مالاً ؛ لأنه لم يحتبس بمالها. انتهى. لا يخفى ما فيه من الإيجار المخل، فإنه يفيد أنه إذا أخذ مالاً غير مال المضاربة، وأقام بالكوفة لا نفقةً له وليس كذلك، فالمقصود ما ذكرناه نقلاً عن «المبسوط»، وغيره.
[٣] قوله : في ماله ؛ لأنه لم يحتبس نفسه لأجل المضاربة، بل هو ساكن بالمسكن الأصلي.
[٤] قوله : كدوائه ؛ فإنّه في ماله مطلقاً، سواء كان في الحضر أو في السفر.

وفي سفره طعامه وشرابه وكسوتُه ، وأجرةً خادمِه، وغسل ثيابه، والدهنُ في موضع يحتاج إليه
نفقة المضارب : مبتدأ، وفي ماله خبره ، وإن مرضَ المضارب سواء كان في المصر، أو في السفر، فإنَّ الدَّواءَ في ماله ، وعن أبي حنيفة له الدواء بمنزلة النفقة". (وفي سفره طعامه وشرابه ، وكسوتُه ، وأجرة خادمه، وغسل ثيابه، والدهنُ في موضع يحتاج إليه)
[ ١ ] قوله : فالدواء في ماله ؛ وهذا ظاهر الرواية، ووجهه : إنَّ الحاجة إلى النفقةُ معلومة الوقوع قطعاً، وإلى الدواء بعارض المرض، فإنه قد يمرض وقد لا يمرض، فلم يثبت كونه لازماً ؛ ولذا نفقة المرأة كانت على الزوج ودواؤها في مالها. (۲) قوله : بمنزلة النفقة ؛ فإنَّ الدواء لإصلاح البدن، والمضارب لا يقدر على التجارة إلا به فصار كالنفقة، وكذلك النورة والرهن في قولهما، وقال محمد ه : الرهن في المال، وروى الحسن الله أنه إذا احتجم أو أطلى، أو اختضب أو أكل فاكهة مثل ما يصنع التجار، فذاك من مال المضاربة. ذكره العيني)، وغيره.
[٣) قوله : وفي سفره ؛ عطف على قوله : في مصره ؛ أي ونفقة مضارب عمل في سفره، وأراد بالسفر ما لا يكون حضراً ولا موضعاً اتخذه دارا توطن به ، حتى لو نوى الإقامة في مصر خمسة عشر يوماً ولم يتخذه وطناً فنفقته في مال المضاربة. كما صرحوا به
[٤]قوله : وأجرة خادمه ؛ أي خابزه ، وطابخه، وغاسلُ ثيابه، وعامل ما لا بُدَّ له منه. كما في «الكرماني»، فقوله : وغسل ثيابه، مستدرك اللهُم إلا أن يراد به ثمن ما يغسل به مثل الحرض والصابون. كما في «الكفاية». كذا في «جامع الرموز»(٢ : ١٤٣)، والحق به أجرة الحمام والحلاق. كذا في «المنح (۳).
[0] قوله : وغسل ثيابه.... الخ ؛ فإنّ الشخص إذا كان طويل الشعر وسيخ الثياب يعد من الصعاليك، ويقل معاملوه ، فصار ما به تكثرُ الرغبات في المعاملة معه من جملة النفقة، وكل ما يعين المضارب على العمل، أو يخدم دابته، فنفقتهم كنفقته.
Section on what the mudarib does. And the expense of a mudarib who works in his city is from his own wealth, like his medicine. [Commentary:] (1) His statement: 'And expense of a mudarib' means a valid mudarabah, for the mudarib in an invalid mudarabah is not entitled to expenses because he is a hired worker, as they explicitly stated. (2) His statement: 'in his city' – al-Birjandi said in Sharh al-Niqayah: 'By 'his city' is meant the city in which he resides, whether it is his original homeland or not. If the mudarabah contract took place in Kufa, and it is not his original homeland, then as long as he is there, his expenses are from his own wealth. If he travels and returns to Kufa for his trade, then his expenses are from the mudarabah capital.' Thus in al-Khazanah. End. And he said in al-Mabsut: 'If someone gives him capital as a mudarabah while both are in Kufa, and Kufa is not the mudarib's homeland, he does not spend on himself from the capital as long as he is in Kufa, because his stay there is not for the mudarabah, so he does not deserve expenses until he leaves for his homeland. Then if he returns to Kufa for his trade, he spends in Kufa from the mudarabah capital because his residence there was borrowed and was broken by travel. So his return after that to Kufa and his going to another city are the same. If he marries a woman and takes her as a homeland, his expenses from the mudarabah capital cease because his stay after marriage is for his family, akin to his original homeland.' Mentioned by al-Tahtawi (1) quoting from Maliki, and likewise in al-Bahr (2) quoting from al-Bada'i, al-Muhit, and al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya. What is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'If he intends to reside in a city but does not take it as a home, he has expenses as long as he does not take money (from the mudarabah) because he has not detained himself for its sake.' End. What is in it of misleading ambiguity is not hidden, for it implies that if he takes money other than the mudarabah capital and stays in Kufa, he has no expenses – and that is not so. The intent is what we have mentioned quoting from al-Mabsut and others. (3) His statement: 'from his own wealth' because he has not detained himself for the sake of the mudarabah, rather he is resident in his original home. (4) His statement: 'like his medicine' – it is from his own wealth absolutely, whether in residence or travel. [Page 388] And during his travel: his food, drink, clothing, wages of his servant, washing of his clothes, and oil in a place where it is needed. [Commentary:] (1) His statement: 'the medicine is from his wealth' – this is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), and its reasoning: the need for expenses is certainly known to occur, while for medicine it is due to the accident of illness, for he may become ill or not, so it is not established as necessary. Hence the wife's expenses were upon the husband and her medicine upon her own wealth. (2) His statement: 'like the expense' – that is because medicine is for the repair of the body, and the mudarib cannot engage in trade without it, so it becomes like expenses. Likewise, shaving (nurah) and cupping (rahn) in the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad al-Shaybani said: Cupping (rahn) is from the capital. And al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reported that if he undergoes cupping (hijamah) or depilation (atla), or dyes (iktadaba), or eats fruit like merchants do, then that is from the mudarabah capital. Mentioned by al-Ayni and others. (3) His statement: 'And during his travel' – it is a conjunction to his statement 'in his city', i.e., the expense of a mudarib who works during his travel. He intended by travel what is neither residence nor a place he has taken as a home to settle in, so that even if he intends to stay in a city for fifteen days but does not take it as a home, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital, as they explicitly stated. (4) His statement: 'and wages of his servant' – i.e., his baker, cook, washer of his clothes, and the one who does what is indispensable for him, as in al-Kirmani. So his statement 'and washing of his clothes' is redundant, unless what is meant is the price of what is used for washing, like potash and soap, as in al-Kifayah. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz (2:143), and to this is added the fee of the bathhouse and barber, as in al-Minh. (0) His statement: 'and washing of his clothes... etc.' – for a person with long hair and dirty clothes is considered among the vagabonds, and his business partners decrease. So what increases desire for dealing with him is part of expenses. And everything that helps the mudarib in his work, or serves his animal, their expenses are like his expenses.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, expanding the ruling on the mudarib's expenses. The core ruling: the mudarib's personal living expenses when he is in his own city (his established residence) are from his own personal wealth, not from the mudarabah capital. This includes medicine, even if he falls ill while traveling. However, when the mudarib is traveling for the purpose of the mudarabah, all necessary expenses — food, drink, clothing, servant wages, laundry, oil, etc. — are from the mudarabah capital. The commentary clarifies what counts as 'his city': it is the place where he resides, regardless of whether it is his original homeland. If he resides in a place temporarily for the mudarabah, then his expenses initially are from his own wealth, but if he leaves and returns for trade, they become from the capital. If he marries and settles in a new place, it becomes his new homeland, and expenses revert to his own wealth. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement: the majority (zahir al-riwaya) holds that medicine is from the mudarib's own money, while Abu Hanifa considers it like other expenses, from the capital, because it is necessary for him to maintain his ability to trade. The commentary prefers the zahir al-riwaya position, but acknowledges the alternative view.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The active partner in a mudarabah contract who invests labor/effort in the capital provided by the rabb al-mal.
نفقة (nafaqah): Living expenses including food, clothing, shelter, and other necessities.
مصر (misr): A city or town where the mudarib has established residence, whether original or adopted as a homeland.
سفر (safar): Travel for the purposes of the mudarabah, excluding residence in a place taken as a home.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon position (zahir al-riwaya), from whose wealth does the mudarib's medicine come if he falls ill while traveling?
A) From the mudarabah capital, like food and drink.
B) From his own personal wealth.
C) From the rabb al-mal's personal wealth.
D) It is shared equally between the mudarib and the rabb al-mal.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's expenses in his city are personal; during travel, they are partnership expenses, with the exception of medicine which remains personal.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
advanced
10 min
The Mudarib's Medicine and Travel Expenses: Intra-Hanafi Dispute Analysis
Expands the ruling on mudarib's expenses with intra-Hanafi dispute on medicine

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to evaluate the intra-Hanafi dispute regarding whether the mudarib's medicine is from personal wealth or from the mudarabah capital, and apply the relied-upon position.

Core Idea
Medicine is a contested expense: Abu Hanifa treats it as partnership cost, but the majority requires personal payment.

While the general rule for the mudarib's expenses is straightforward, the treatment of medicine reveals a subtle intra-school debate. Abu Hanifa analogized medicine to food, arguing it is necessary for trade. The majority, however, distinguished based on certainty of need. This lesson unpacks that dispute and also clarifies additional travel expenses like servant wages and laundry.

Guided Path
1
Identify the point of dispute
Whether the mudarib's medicine expenses are from his own money or from the mudarabah capital. The matn states medicine is like expenses (i.e., personal), but there is a narrative from Abu Hanifa that it is akin to expenses (from capital).
The dispute is only about medicine; other expenses in travel are unanimously from capital.
2
Analyze the majority's proof
The need for expenses (food, etc.) is certain to occur; need for medicine is contingent (may not happen). Therefore, medicine is not a necessary expense of the mudarabah.
Analogy: wife's expenses are on husband, but her medicine is from her own wealth.
3
Analyze Abu Hanifa's proof
Medicine is needed to repair the body so the mudarib can trade, similar to food. So it should be from the capital.
This is a qiyas (analogy) on the basis of necessity.
4
Understand the tarjih (preference)
The commentator declares 'هذا ظاهر الرواية' (this is the apparent transmission), indicating the majority view is the relied-upon position.
Additional narrations: Abu Yusuf and Muhammad agree with the majority; Muhammad differs on cupping (rahn) which he says is from capital.
5
Apply the rule to other travel expenses
Travel expenses from capital include food, drink, clothing, servant wages, laundry, oil. Also bathhouse and barber fees are included.
The rationale is to maintain the mudarib's professional appearance to attract business partners.
