Section on what the mudarib does. And the expense of a mudarib who works in his city is from his own wealth, like his medicine. [Commentary:] (1) His statement: 'And expense of a mudarib' means a valid mudarabah, for the mudarib in an invalid mudarabah is not entitled to expenses because he is a hired worker, as they explicitly stated. (2) His statement: 'in his city' – al-Birjandi said in Sharh al-Niqayah: 'By 'his city' is meant the city in which he resides, whether it is his original homeland or not. If the mudarabah contract took place in Kufa, and it is not his original homeland, then as long as he is there, his expenses are from his own wealth. If he travels and returns to Kufa for his trade, then his expenses are from the mudarabah capital.' Thus in al-Khazanah. End. And he said in al-Mabsut: 'If someone gives him capital as a mudarabah while both are in Kufa, and Kufa is not the mudarib's homeland, he does not spend on himself from the capital as long as he is in Kufa, because his stay there is not for the mudarabah, so he does not deserve expenses until he leaves for his homeland. Then if he returns to Kufa for his trade, he spends in Kufa from the mudarabah capital because his residence there was borrowed and was broken by travel. So his return after that to Kufa and his going to another city are the same. If he marries a woman and takes her as a homeland, his expenses from the mudarabah capital cease because his stay after marriage is for his family, akin to his original homeland.' Mentioned by al-Tahtawi (1) quoting from Maliki, and likewise in al-Bahr (2) quoting from al-Bada'i, al-Muhit, and al-Fatawa al-Zahiriyya. What is in al-Durr al-Mukhtar: 'If he intends to reside in a city but does not take it as a home, he has expenses as long as he does not take money (from the mudarabah) because he has not detained himself for its sake.' End. What is in it of misleading ambiguity is not hidden, for it implies that if he takes money other than the mudarabah capital and stays in Kufa, he has no expenses – and that is not so. The intent is what we have mentioned quoting from al-Mabsut and others. (3) His statement: 'from his own wealth' because he has not detained himself for the sake of the mudarabah, rather he is resident in his original home. (4) His statement: 'like his medicine' – it is from his own wealth absolutely, whether in residence or travel. [Page 388] And during his travel: his food, drink, clothing, wages of his servant, washing of his clothes, and oil in a place where it is needed. [Commentary:] (1) His statement: 'the medicine is from his wealth' – this is the apparent transmission (zahir al-riwaya), and its reasoning: the need for expenses is certainly known to occur, while for medicine it is due to the accident of illness, for he may become ill or not, so it is not established as necessary. Hence the wife's expenses were upon the husband and her medicine upon her own wealth. (2) His statement: 'like the expense' – that is because medicine is for the repair of the body, and the mudarib cannot engage in trade without it, so it becomes like expenses. Likewise, shaving (nurah) and cupping (rahn) in the view of the two (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad). And Muhammad al-Shaybani said: Cupping (rahn) is from the capital. And al-Hasan ibn Ziyad reported that if he undergoes cupping (hijamah) or depilation (atla), or dyes (iktadaba), or eats fruit like merchants do, then that is from the mudarabah capital. Mentioned by al-Ayni and others. (3) His statement: 'And during his travel' – it is a conjunction to his statement 'in his city', i.e., the expense of a mudarib who works during his travel. He intended by travel what is neither residence nor a place he has taken as a home to settle in, so that even if he intends to stay in a city for fifteen days but does not take it as a home, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital, as they explicitly stated. (4) His statement: 'and wages of his servant' – i.e., his baker, cook, washer of his clothes, and the one who does what is indispensable for him, as in al-Kirmani. So his statement 'and washing of his clothes' is redundant, unless what is meant is the price of what is used for washing, like potash and soap, as in al-Kifayah. Thus in Jami' al-Rumuz (2:143), and to this is added the fee of the bathhouse and barber, as in al-Minh. (0) His statement: 'and washing of his clothes... etc.' – for a person with long hair and dirty clothes is considered among the vagabonds, and his business partners decrease. So what increases desire for dealing with him is part of expenses. And everything that helps the mudarib in his work, or serves his animal, their expenses are like his expenses.
Source pages p. 387

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on the matn of al-Wiqayah, expanding the ruling on the mudarib's expenses. The core ruling: the mudarib's personal living expenses when he is in his own city (his established residence) are from his own personal wealth, not from the mudarabah capital. This includes medicine, even if he falls ill while traveling. However, when the mudarib is traveling for the purpose of the mudarabah, all necessary expenses — food, drink, clothing, servant wages, laundry, oil, etc. — are from the mudarabah capital. The commentary clarifies what counts as 'his city': it is the place where he resides, regardless of whether it is his original homeland. If he resides in a place temporarily for the mudarabah, then his expenses initially are from his own wealth, but if he leaves and returns for trade, they become from the capital. If he marries and settles in a new place, it becomes his new homeland, and expenses revert to his own wealth. The passage also presents an intra-Hanafi disagreement: the majority (zahir al-riwaya) holds that medicine is from the mudarib's own money, while Abu Hanifa considers it like other expenses, from the capital, because it is necessary for him to maintain his ability to trade. The commentary prefers the zahir al-riwaya position, but acknowledges the alternative view.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudarib): The active partner in a mudarabah contract who invests labor/effort in the capital provided by the rabb al-mal.
نفقة (nafaqah): Living expenses including food, clothing, shelter, and other necessities.
دواء (dawa): Medicine, treatment for illness.
نورة (nurah): Shaving or depilation, a form of grooming.
رهن (rahn): Cupping, a medical treatment.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the relied-upon Hanafi position, from which source should the mudarib pay for medicine while traveling?
A) From the mudarabah capital, like food.
B) From his own personal wealth, because need is not certain.
C) From the rabb al-mal's personal wealth.
D) Shared equally between mudarib and rabb al-mal.

Key Takeaway
Medicine is an exception: even during travel, it is a personal expense according to the relied-upon Hanafi position, due to the uncertainty of the need.

Medicine for the Mudārib: Own Expense or Capital?
Intra-Hanafi dispute on mudārib's medicine expense

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah position on medicine for a mudārib and explain the reasoning behind the alternative narration from Abu Hanifa.

Core Idea
Medicine for the mudārib is from his own money, not the capital.

This passage examines a key intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the mudārib's medical expenses. While daily expenses (nafaqah) are covered by the mudārabah capital, the well-known position (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah) holds that medicine is the mudārib's personal liability. An alternative narration from Abu Hanifa equates medicine with expenses, sparking a debate about the nature of necessity and certainty in financial arrangements.

Guided Path
1
Identify the central ruling
First, note the ruling from Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah: medicine is from the mudārib's own wealth, regardless of location.
This is the muftā bihī position.
2
Analyze the rationale (ta‘līl)
The text gives a reason: need for expense is certain, need for medicine is contingent on illness. Compare with the wife's analogy.
Certainty vs. contingency is the key distinction.
3
Explore the alternative narration
Abu Hanifa's view: medicine is like expense because it restores the body for trade. Extend to lime (nūrah) and cupping.
Note the differences among Abu Yusuf, Muhammad, and al-Hasan.
4
Determine the operative position
The muftā bihī is Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah. Later Hanafi fatwa relies on this; the other narrations are considered weaker (nawādir).
Always check authority weight when citing Hanafi rulings.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وإن مرض المضارب سواء كان في المصر أو في السفر، فإن الدواء في ماله. وعن أبي حنيفة له الدواء بمنزلة النفقة. قوله: فالدواء في ماله؛ وهذا ظاهر الرواية، ووجهه: إن الحاجة إلى النفقة معلومة الوقوع قطعاً، وإلى الدواء بعارض المرض، فإنه قد يمرض وقد لا يمرض، فلم يثبت كونه لازماً؛ ولذا نفقة المرأة كانت على الزوج ودواؤها في مالها. قوله: بمنزلة النفقة؛ فإن الدواء لإصلاح البدن، والمضارب لا يقدر على التجارة إلا به فصار كالنفقة، وكذلك النورة والرهن في قولهما، وقال محمد: الرهن في المال، وروى الحسن أنه إذا احتجم أو أطلى، أو اختضب أو أكل فاكهة مثل ما يصنع التجار، فذاك من مال المضاربة.
And if the mudārib falls ill, whether he is in the city or in travel, then medicine is from his own wealth. And from Abu Hanifa: for him medicine is in the status of expense. His statement: 'for medicine is from his wealth': this is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah, and its rationale is that the need for expense is known to occur definitely, while the need for medicine is due to an incidental illness, for he may fall ill or may not fall ill, so it is not established as necessary. Hence the wife's expense is on the husband while her medicine is from her own wealth. His statement: 'in the status of expense': because medicine is for the repair of the body, and the mudārib cannot engage in trade without it, so it becomes like expense. And similarly lime (for depilation) and cupping according to their (Abu Yusuf and Muhammad's) saying. And Muhammad said: cupping is from the capital. And al-Hasan narrated: if he performs cupping, or uses depilatory, or dyes, or eats fruit like what merchants do, then that is from the mudārabah capital.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is an intra-Hanafi dispute regarding the cost of medicine for a mudārib who falls ill. The Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah (the well-known transmission) states that medicine is from the mudārib's own money, whether he is in the city or traveling. The reasoning is that the need for daily expenses (nafaqah) is certain whereas the need for medicine is contingent on illness, which is not guaranteed. The analogy with a wife's expenses (from husband) and her medicine (from herself) supports this. However, there is a narration from Abu Hanifa that medicine is similar to expense (nafaqah) because it is necessary for the mudārib to repair his body to continue trading. Abu Yusuf and Muhammad (al-Shaybani) extend this to include depilatory lime and cupping, but Muhammad reportedly says cupping is from the capital. Also, a narration from al-Hasan (ibn Ziyād) includes cupping, depilatory, dye, and even eating fruit if done in the manner of merchants. This shows the range of opinions within the Hanafi school on what counts as necessary expense.

Key Terms
ظاهر الرواية (Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah): The well-known and authoritative transmissions from the Hanafi imams, compiled by Muhammad al-Shaybani in six books.
النورة (al-nūrah): A depilatory paste (lime and arsenic) used for hair removal.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
What is the Ẓāhir al-Riwāyah ruling on medicine for a sick mudārib?
A) Medicine is from the mudārabah capital.
B) Medicine is from the mudārib's own wealth.
C) Medicine is only from capital if the mudārib is traveling.
D) Medicine is from the investor's wealth.

Key Takeaway
In the Hanafi school, the mudārib's medicine is a personal expense, not a partnership expense, due to the contingent nature of illness.

Mudārib's Travel Expenses: Ruling and Proof
Ruling on mudārib's travel expenses from mudārabah capital

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to state the Hanafi ruling on the mudārib's travel expenses and explain the qiyas-based proof (analogy to a judge's salary and wife's maintenance).

Core Idea
Necessary travel expenses of the mudārib are from the mudārabah capital, as he is confined for the work.

In a mudārabah partnership, the mudārib (agent) often travels for trade. A key question is who bears his food, clothing, and other expenses. The Hanafi school holds that these are from the capital during travel, analogizing the mudārib to a judge confined for public service or a wife confined in her husband's home. This lesson unpacks the ruling and its rational proof.

Guided Path
1
Identify the act and its ruling
The mudārib's expenditure on food, drink, clothing, servant, laundry, and oil during travel is permissible (mubah) from the mudārabah capital.
This is not an obligation but a right; the mudārib may spend reasonably.
2
Locate the dalil
The proof is qiyas: the mudārib, while traveling for the mudārabah, is confined (maḥbūs) for the work, similar to a judge whose salary is from the treasury and a wife whose nafaqah is from her husband.
The common denominator is confinement for another's benefit.
3
Trace the wajh al-dalalah
The effective cause (illah) is the confinement for the work. Just as the judge's and wife's expenses are borne by the party who benefits from their confinement, so the mudārib's expenses are borne by the capital.
The capital is the benefit of the investor (rabb al-māl) and the mudārib.
4
Note the ikhtilaf
The Shafi‘i and Hanbali schools hold that travel expenses are from the mudārib's own money, as he is a partner in profit. The Hanafi view is mufta bihi.
The practical difference is significant in accounting and profit distribution.
5
Evaluate the Hanafi tarjih
The reliance on qiyas is strong; the mudārib's confinement is more intense during travel, and the capital directly benefits from his efforts.
Later Hanafi works confirm this as the relied-upon position.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وفي سفره طعامه وشرابه وكسوتُه، وأجرةُ خادمِه، وغسل ثيابه، والدهنُ في موضع يحتاج إليه. نفقة المضارب: مبتدأ، وفي ماله خبره. قوله: وفي سفره؛ عطف على قوله: في مصره؛ أي ونفقة مضارب عمل في سفره، وأراد بالسفر ما لا يكون حضراً ولا موضعاً اتخذه داراً توطن به، حتى لو نوى الإقامة في مصر خمسة عشر يوماً ولم يتخذه وطناً فنفقته في مال المضاربة. كما صرحوا به. قوله: وأجرة خادمه؛ أي خابزه، وطابخه، وغاسل ثيابه، وعامل ما لا بد له منه. كما في «الكرماني»، فقوله: وغسل ثيابه، مستدرك اللهم إلا أن يراد به ثمن ما يغسل به مثل الحرض والصابون. كما في «الكفاية». كذا في «جامع الرموز». والحق به أجرة الحمام والحلاق. كذا في «المنح». قوله: وغسل ثيابه... الخ؛ فإن الشخص إذا كان طويل الشعر وسيخ الثياب يعد من الصعاليك، ويقل معاملوه، فصار ما به تكثر الرغبات في المعاملة معه من جملة النفقة، وكل ما يعين المضارب على العمل، أو يخدم دابته، فنفقته كنفقته. قوله: كالحجاز؛ فإن أرض الحجاز حارة يحتاج أهلها إلى ترطيب أبدانهم بالدهن. كذا في «البناية».
And during his travel, his food, drink, clothing, the hire of his servant, washing of his clothes, and oil in a place where it is needed. 'The expense of the mudārib': a subject, and 'in its capital' is its predicate. His statement: 'And during his travel' is conjuncted to his statement: 'in his city'; meaning the expense of a mudārib who works during his travel, and by 'travel' he means what is not a residence nor a place adopted as a domicile to settle in, such that if he intends to stay in a city for fifteen days but does not adopt it as a domicile, his expense is from the mudārabah capital, as they explicitly stated. His statement: 'And the hire of his servant' meaning his baker, cook, washer of his clothes, and the doer of what is indispensable for him, as in 'al-Kirmānī'. His statement: 'washing of his clothes' is redundant unless what is intended is the price of what is used for washing like fuller's earth and soap, as in 'al-Kifāyah'. So also in 'Jāmi' al-Rumūz'. And joined to it is the fee of the bathhouse and the barber, as in 'al-Minaḥ'. His statement: 'washing of his clothes' etc.: because if a person has long hair and shabby clothes, he is considered among the vagabonds, and his dealers will be few. So what increases desire for dealing with him becomes part of the expense. And everything that helps the mudārib in his work, or serves his animal, its expense is like his expense. His statement: 'like the Hijaz': because the land of Hijaz is hot, its people need to moisten their bodies with oil. So in 'al-Bināyah'.
Source pages p. 388

Commentary
This passage is a ruling statement with proof, explaining what expenses of the mudārib during travel are borne by the mudārabah capital. The passage begins by listing the items: food, drink, clothing, servant's hire, laundry, and oil where needed. The commentator clarifies that 'travel' means being away from one's usual place of residence; even if staying fifteen days in a city without adopting it as a home, expenses are from the capital. The servant includes necessary workers like baker and cook. Laundry cost may refer to soap and other washing agents. The rationale is that a presentable appearance (clean clothes, groomed hair) attracts business partners; hence such expenses are necessary for the mudārib's work. The commentator also extends similar expenses to the mudārib's animal. The example of Hijaz shows that oil for the body in a hot climate is a genuine need. Thus all these are included in the mudārib's travel expenses, which are from the mudārabah capital.

Key Terms
مضارب (muḍārib): The agent in a mudārabah (qirāḍ) contract who manages the capital provided by the investor (rabb al-māl).
نفقة (nafaqah): Maintenance expenses including food, drink, clothing, and other necessities.

Key Terms from Source Passage

A) His own personal wealth.
B) The mudārabah capital.
C) The investor's personal wealth.
D) The profit share.

Key Takeaway
The mudārib's necessary travel expenses are from the mudārabah capital based on the qiyas of confinement, and this is the mufta bihi position in the Hanafi school.

Travel Expenses of the Mudārib: Capital or Personal?
Hanafi vs. Shafi'i/Ahmad on mudārib travel expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain why the Hanafi school charges the mudārib's travel expenses to the mudārabah capital and evaluate the Shafi'i/Ahmad counter-argument.

Core Idea
Expenses are for confinement; a traveling mudārib is confined for the business, so capital pays.

When a mudārib (agent) travels for the mudārabah business, who pays for his food and lodging? The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools say the mudārib pays from his own pocket, since he earns a profit share. The Hanafi school, however, rules that the capital bears these expenses. This lesson unpacks the Hanafi proof based on the principle of ihtibās (confinement) and the key distinctions that set the mudārib apart from a hired worker or an ordinary partner.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi position
Identify the ruling: the mudārib's travel expenses are taken from the mudārabah capital, not from his personal wealth.
This is the muftā bihi position in the Hanafi school.
2
Identify the opposing view
Recognize that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that expenses are from the mudārib's own wealth because he is compensated by a profit share.
They analogize to the mudārib's status in the city, where his expenses are personal.
3
Follow the Hanafi proof: ihtibās (confinement)
Analyze the Hanafi analogy: expenses are obligatory due to ihtibās, as seen with a judge (confined for public service → salary from treasury) and a wife (confined in husband's house → maintenance from husband). The mudārib in the city is not confined because he lives at home, but traveling confines him for the work, so the capital must provide.
The operative cause (illah) is ihtibās, not the existence of compensation.
4
Examine the distinctions
Contrast the mudārib with a hired worker (ajīr) who has a guaranteed wage and thus no hardship, and with a regular partner whose custom is not to spend from the other's capital. These distinctions reinforce the Hanafi position.
The mudārib's profit share is uncertain; this uncertainty makes him reliant on capital for travel costs.
5
Determine the practical consequence (thamarah)
Shafi'i/Ahmad: mudārib deducts expenses from his own share (or pays out of pocket). Hanafi: expenses are taken from the capital before profit calculation, reducing the investor's capital but protecting the mudārib from loss.
This affects profit distribution and the investor's risk.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وقال الشافعي وأحمد: نفقته في السفر في مال نفسه؛ لأنه يستحق على عمله أجزاء من الربح، فكان كالحضر. ولنا: إن النفقة إنما تجب للاحتباس كنفقة القاضي في بيت المال؛ لاحتباسه لمصالح المسلمين، ونفقة المرأة في مال الزوج؛ لاحتباسها في منزله، والمضارب في المصر ساكن بالمسكن الأصلي، فلا يكون محبوساً لعمل المضاربة، وإذا سافر لها صار محبوساً لعملها، فوجبت نفقته في مالها. بخلاف الأجير فإنه يعمل بأجر معلوم الحصول، فلا يتضرر بالإنفاق من مال نفسه، والمضارب يعمل بجزء من الربح، وهو قد يحصل وقد لا يحصل، وبخلاف الشريك؛ لأن العادة أن الشريك العامل لا ينفق على نفسه من مال الشريك الآخر. كذا في «شرح الوافي»، وقال في «النهاية»: الشريك إذا سافر بمال الشركة فنفقته في ذلك المال، روي ذلك عن محمد.
And al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: His expense during travel is from his own wealth because he is entitled to portions of the profit for his work, so he is like when he is in the city. And our proof: Expenses are only obligatory due to confinement, like the salary of a judge from the public treasury because of his confinement for the interests of the Muslims, and the wife's expense from the husband's wealth because of her confinement in his house. The mudārib when in the city resides in his original dwelling, so he is not confined for the work of mudārabah. But when he travels for it, he becomes confined for its work, so his expense is obligatory from its capital. Unlike a hired worker (ajīr) because he works for a known wage that is certain to be obtained, so he is not harmed by spending from his own wealth. The mudārib works for a share of profit, which may be obtained and may not. And unlike a partner because the custom is that the working partner does not spend on himself from the other partner's wealth. So in 'Sharḥ al-Wāfī', and he said in 'al-Nihāyah': A partner who travels with the partnership capital, his expense is from that capital – that is narrated from Muhammad.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the source of the mudārib's travel expenses. Imam al-Shafi'i and Imam Ahmad hold that the mudārib's expenses during travel are from his own personal wealth, because he receives a share of profit as compensation for his work, similar to the situation in the city. The Hanafi response is that expenses are due to the mudārib being confined (iḥtibās) for the work. They draw analogies: a judge's salary from the treasury because he is confined for public service, and a wife's maintenance from her husband because she is confined in his home. The mudārib in the city is not confined as he lives at home, but when he travels for the mudārabah, he is confined for its sake, so his expenses should be from the mudārabah capital. Furthermore, the Hanafis distinguish the mudārib from a hired worker (ajīr), who receives a guaranteed wage and can afford to spend his own money, and from a regular partner, who by custom does not spend from the other's money. However, they note a narration from Muhammad that a partner who travels with partnership property can have his expenses from the partnership capital. This shows the internal consistency of Hanafi reasoning: confinement is the key factor.

Key Terms
احتباس (iḥtibās): Confinement or detention; the state of being held for a specific purpose, which in Hanafi fiqh creates entitlement to expenses.
أجير (ajīr): A hired worker who receives a fixed wage for his labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi madhhab, why are the travel expenses of a mudārib from the mudārabah capital?
A) Because the mudārib is poor.
B) Because the mudārib is confined for the work during travel.
C) Because the investor is obligated to provide for the mudārib.
D) Because the mudārib's profit share is guaranteed.

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi fiqh, the mudārib's travel expenses are charged to the capital because confinement (ihtibās) during travel creates an obligation, unlike a guaranteed-wage worker or a partner by custom.

Mudarib's Travel Expenses and the Shafi'i Dispute
Inter-madhhab dispute on mudarib travel expenses

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to explain the Hanafi rationale (ihtibas) for the mudarib's travel expenses from the capital and contrast it with the Shafi'i/Hanbali position, identifying the thamarah al-khilaf.

Core Idea
Expenses are compensation for confinement (ihtibas), so the traveling mudarib is entitled to them from the capital.

This passage addresses a classic inter-madhhab dispute: when a mudarib travels for the mudarabah business, should his reasonable expenses (like food, transport, fodder) come from the capital or from his own pocket? The Hanafi school rules that they come from the capital, based on the principle that expenses are owed for confinement (ihtibas). The Shafi'i and Hanbali schools disagree, analogizing the mudarib to a wage worker. By studying this dispute, you will sharpen your ability to analyze competing analogies and identify the practical consequences of each school's view.

Guided Path
1
State the Hanafi ruling and its conditions
Identify the mudarib's entitlement to reasonable travel expenses from the capital, and note that any excess must be guaranteed by him. Leftover provisions upon return must be returned to the capital.
The matn explicitly states: 'His riding animal, its hire and purchase, and its fodder [are] from its capital in a reasonable manner, and he guarantees the excess.'
2
Identify the opponent's position and their dalil
Recognize that al-Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that travel expenses are from the mudarib's own wealth because he earns a profit share, likening him to a worker in the city.
The text says: 'because he is entitled to shares of the profit for his work, so it is like being in the city.'
3
Analyze the Hanafi proof (istihsan based on ihtibas)
Examine the Hanafi argument: expenses are compensation for confinement (ihtibas). The mudarib is not confined when in his city, but upon traveling he becomes confined for the mudarabah work. This is supported by analogies to a judge (expenses from treasury) and a wife (maintenance from husband).
The key phrase: 'إنَّ النفقة إنما تجب للاحتباس' (Expenses are only obligatory for confinement).
4
Evaluate the Hanafi rebuttal of the opponent's analogy
Understand how the Hanafis distinguish the mudarib from a hired worker (ajir) with a fixed wage, and from a business partner (sharik) where custom dictates no mutual expense coverage. The mudarib's profit share is uncertain, so he would suffer hardship if forced to pay his own expenses.
The distinction from ajir is crucial: the ajir's wage is certain, so he is not harmed by spending from his own wealth; the mudarib's profit is uncertain.
5
Determine the thamarah al-khilaf (practical consequence)
Conclude that in the Hanafi school, the mudarib deducts travel expenses from the capital before profit distribution; in the Shafi'i school, he bears them personally. This difference directly affects the mudarib's net profit calculation.
This is a clear example of how a difference in legal reasoning yields a different practical outcome.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
كتاب المضاربة المضارب الذي يضارب [٢] وركوبه كراء وشراء، وعلفُه في مالها بالمعروف وضَمِنَ الفضل ، وردُّ ما بقي في يده بعد قدوم مصره إلى مالها كالحجاز ) ، ( وركوبه (۳) كراء وشراء، وعلفه في مالها بالمعروف وضَمِنَ الفضل) : أي إن أنفق زائداً على المعروف ضَمِنَ الفضل، (ورد ما بقي" في يده بعد قدوم مصره إلى مالها : أي ما بقي من الطعام ونحوه. 11 ] قوله : كالحجاز؛ فإنَّ أرض الحجاز حارّةً يحتاج أهلها إلى ترطيب أبدانهم بالدهن. كذا في «البناية»(۳). [٢] قوله : في مالها ؛ أي في مال المضاربة، سواء كان المالُ قليلاً أو كثيراً ؛ لأنه حبس نفسه لأجلها، فالنفقةُ جزاء الاحتباس ، وقال الشافعي وأحمد ه : نفقته في السفر في مال نفسه ؛ لأنه يستحق على عمله أجزاء من الربح ، فكان كالحضر. ولنا : إنَّ النفقة إنما تجب للاحتباس كنفقة القاضي في بيت المال ؛ لاحتباسه المصالح المسلمين، ونفقة المرأة في مال الزوج ؛ لاحتباسها في منزله، والمضارب في المصرٍ ساكن بالمسكن الأصلي، فلا يكون محبوساً لعمل المضاربة، وإذا سافر لها صار محبوساً لعملها، فوجب نفقته في مالها. بخلاف الأجير فإنّه يعملُ بأجرٍ معلوم الحصول، فلا يتضرر بالإنفاق من مال نفسه، والمضارب يعمل بجزء من الربح، وهو قد يحصل، وقد لا يحصل، وبخلافِ الشريك ؛ لأن العادة أن الشريك العامل لا ينفقُ على نفسه من مال الشريك الآخر. كذا في «شرح الوافي»، وقال في «النهاية»: الشريك إذا سافر بمال الشركة فنفقته في ذلك المال، روي ذلك عن محمد . [٣] قوله : ورد ما بقي...الخ ; لأن الاستحقاق قد انتهى بالرجوع إلى مصره، كما أن الحاج من الغير إذا بقي شيء من النفقة في يده بعد رجوعه يرده إلى من حج من جانبه.
Book of Mudarabah: The mudarib who enters into mudarabah. His riding animal, its hire and purchase, and its fodder [are] from its capital in a reasonable manner, and he guarantees the excess. And he returns what remains in his hand after arriving at his city to its capital like the Hijaz. (His riding animal, its hire and purchase, and its fodder from its capital in a reasonable manner, and he guarantees the excess): meaning if he spends more than is reasonable, he guarantees the excess. (And he returns what remains in his hand after arriving at his city to its capital): meaning what remains of food and the like. [11] His saying: like the Hijaz; because the land of Hijaz is hot, its people need to moisturize their bodies with oil. Thus in al-Binayah. [2] His saying: from its capital; meaning from the mudarabah capital, whether the capital is little or much; because he has imprisoned himself for its sake; so the expense is compensation for confinement. Al-Shafi'i and Ahmad said: his expense during travel is from his own wealth, because he is entitled to shares of the profit for his work, so it is like being in the city. And our [Hanafi] argument: expenses are only obligatory for confinement, like the expense of the judge from the treasury due to his confinement for the interests of the Muslims, and the expense of the wife from the husband's wealth due to her confinement in his house. The mudarib in the city resides in his original dwelling, so he is not confined for the work of mudarabah, but when he travels for it, he becomes confined for its work, so his expense becomes obligatory from its capital. Unlike the hired worker, for he works for a known wage, so he is not harmed by spending from his own wealth; the mudarib works for a share of profit, which may be obtained or may not. Unlike the partner, because the custom is that the working partner does not spend on himself from the wealth of the other partner. Thus in Sharh al-Wafi. And it is said in al-Nihayah: if a partner travels with partnership wealth, his expense is from that wealth; that is narrated from Muhammad. [3] His saying: and return what remains...etc.; because the entitlement has ended by returning to his city, just as a pilgrim of another if something of the expense remains in his hand after his return, he returns it to the one on whose behalf he performed Hajj.
Source pages p. 389

Commentary
This passage is an inter-madhhab dispute regarding the mudarib's travel expenses. The commentary opens by quoting the matn: the mudarib's riding animal, hire, and fodder are taken from the mudarabah capital in a reasonable amount; any excess must be guaranteed by the mudarib. Additionally, leftover provisions upon return are returned to the capital. The author then explains the reason for the Hijaz example (to moisturize bodies due to heat). The core dispute: Shafi'i and Ahmad hold that travel expenses come from the mudarib's own pocket because he earns a profit share, analogous to working in the city. The Hanafi response, introduced by 'Lana' (our proof), is that expenses are compensation for confinement (ihtibas). The mudarib in the city is not confined, but upon traveling he is confined for the mudarabah, so expenses fall on the capital. This is supported by analogies: the judge's expenses from the treasury and the wife's maintenance from the husband. The mudarib is distinguished from a hired worker (ajir) who receives a definite wage and thus can spend from his own money without hardship, and from a partner (sharik) where custom dictates no such expense. The passage concludes with a ruling on returning leftover provisions, analogized to a pilgrim returning unused funds.

Key Terms
مضاربة (mudarabah): A partnership where one party (rabb al-mal) provides capital and the other (mudarib) provides labor, sharing profits as agreed.
احتباس (ihtibas): Confinement or dedicating oneself for a purpose; here, the mudarib's confinement for the work of mudarabah.
أجير (ajir): A hired worker who receives a fixed wage for his labor.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
According to the Hanafi school, why does the mudarib receive his travel expenses from the mudarabah capital rather than paying them himself?
A) Because he is a hired worker with a fixed wage.
B) Because he is confined (muhtabas) for the work during travel.
C) Because he is a partner sharing all expenses equally.
D) Because the capital owner is obliged to provide for him.

Key Takeaway
The Hanafi school holds that the mudarib's travel expenses are from the capital because they are compensation for his confinement (ihtibas) during travel, distinguishing him from a hired worker with a fixed wage.

Mudarib's Expenses for Short Outings
Ruling on mudarib expenses for short outings

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to determine whether a mudarib's personal expenses during a short business outing (less than three days) are charged to the mudarabah capital or to himself, based on whether he returns home at night.

Core Idea
A mudarib's expenses are from the capital only if he is confined overnight away from home; otherwise they are his own.

This lesson examines a key ruling from the Hanafi school on mudarib expenses for short outings. The passage distinguishes between two scenarios: one where the mudarib leaves in the morning and does not return for the night (like travel), and one where he returns home nightly (like a local market trip). The ruling has practical implications for how expenses are accounted.

Guided Path
1
Identify the distance threshold
The passage defines 'less than travel' as outings under the travel distance of three days and nights. Determine whether the outing qualifies as dun al-safar.
2
Distinguish the two scenarios
Does the mudarib spend the night away from home (la yabitu bi-ahlihi) or does he return home to sleep (yabitu bi-ahlihi)? This distinction is decisive for the ruling.
3
Apply the expense rule
If he does not return at night, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital because he is confined for the work. If he returns, it is like a city market and expenses are from his own pocket.
4
Understand capital recovery
If the mudarib spends from the capital, the owner first recovers the capital from profits before any profit division. This ensures the capital is intact before sharing profit.
See the commentary on 'fariha ya'khudhu'.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
وما دون سفر يغدو إليه، ولا يبيتُ بأهله كالسفر ، وإن بات كسوق مصره، فإن ربح أخذ رب المال ما أنفق المضارب من رأس ماله ، فإن رابح متاعُها حسب نفقته لا نفقة نفسه (وما دون سفر يغدو إليه، ولا يبيتُ بأهله كالسفر، وإن بات كسوق مصره ، فإن ريحَ أخذَ رَبُّ المالِ ما أنفق المضارب من رأس ماله ) : أي أخذ من الربح ما أنفق المضارب من رأس المال ، حتى يتم رأس المال ، فَإِن فَضَلَ شيءٌ قُسِم " (فإن رابح متاعُها حسب نفقتَهُ لا نفقة نفسه ) : أي إن رابح، وقال : قامَ علي بكذا يُحْسَبُ فيه ما أنفق على المتاعِ من كراء حمله ونحو ذلك، ولا يُحْسَبُ [٤] نفقة المضارب. [١] قوله : وما دون سفر...الخ ؛ يعني إن كان خروج المضارب دون السفر، وهو مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها ، فإن كان بحيث يغدو ويذهب إلى ما دونه، ولا يبيت بأهله ؛ أي لا يكون في جميع الليل عند أهله ، فهو في حكم السفر، فنفقته إن عمل فيه في مالِ المضاربة ؛ لكون خروجه لأجلها، فصار محبوساً لها. وإن كان بحيث يغدو ثم يروح فيبيت بأهله، فحكمه حكم المصر، ويكون كالسوقي، فنفقته في مال نفسه ؛ لأنَّ أهل المصر يتجرون في المصر، ويبيتون في منازلهم. [٢] قوله : أي أخذ الخ؛ حاصله : إنَّ المضارب إذا أنفق من مال المضاربة فريحه يأخذه رب المال حتى يتما رأس ماله كاملاً، فيكون النفقة مصروفه إلى الربح دون رأس المال ، فإذا استوفاه كان ما يبقى بينهما على ما شرط. [۳] قوله : فإن فضل شيء قسم ؛ فإنّ قسمةَ الربح إنما شرعت بعد تسليم رأس المال. (٤) قوله : أي إن رابح ... الخ ؛ يعني إن بايع المضاربُ مرابحة المتاع الذي أنفق عليه ضم جميع ما أنفق على المتاع من أجرة الحمال والطراز والسمسار والصباغ وغير ذلك مما تقدم في باب المرابحة إلى رأس المال، بأن يقول : قام علي هكذا ؛ لأن هذه الأشياء تزيد في القيمة. والـعـرف جار بإلحاقها برأس المال في بيع المرابحة، فجاز ذلك، ولا يضم ما أنفقه على نفسيه في سفر إلى رأس المال ؛ لأنَّ التجار لم يتعارفوا بضمه إلى رأس المال، ولا
And what is less than traveling: if he goes early and does not spend the night with his family, it is like traveling; if he spends the night, it is like the market of his city. If there is profit, the capital owner takes what the mudarib spent from his capital. If he sells merchandise on a cost-plus basis, his expense is counted, not his own expense. (What is less than traveling: if he goes early and does not spend the night with his family, it is like traveling; if he spends the night, it is like the market of his city. Then if there is profit, the capital owner takes what the mudarib spent from his capital): meaning he takes from the profit what the mudarib spent from the capital, until the capital is complete. If anything remains, it is divided. (If he sells merchandise on a cost-plus basis, his expense is counted, not his own expense): meaning if he sells on cost-plus, and says: it cost me such-and-such, what he spent on the merchandise like hire for carrying and the like is counted, but the mudarib's own expense is not counted. [1] His saying: 'what is less than traveling...etc.'; meaning if the mudarib's outing is less than traveling, which is a distance of three days and nights, then if he goes early and goes to that distance and does not spend the night with his family (i.e., he is not with his family at all during the night), it is treated as travel, so his expense for working in it is from the mudarabah capital, because his outing is for its sake, so he becomes confined to it. But if he goes early and then returns and spends the night with his family, it is treated as in the city, and he is like a market-goer, so his expense is from his own wealth, because city dwellers trade in the city and sleep in their homes. [2] His saying: 'that is, he takes...etc.'; the summary: if the mudarib spends from the mudarabah capital, the profit is taken by the capital owner until the capital is complete, so the expense is charged to the profit, not the capital. When he has recovered the capital, what remains is divided between them as agreed. [3] His saying: 'if anything remains, it is divided'; because division of profit is only prescribed after delivering the capital. (4) His saying: 'that is, if he sells on cost-plus...etc.'; meaning if the mudarib sells the merchandise on cost-plus on which he spent, he includes all he spent on the merchandise such as the porter's fee, the tailor's fee, the broker's fee, the dyer's fee, and other things as previously mentioned in the chapter of murabahah, into the cost price, by saying: 'it cost me such-and-such'; because these things increase the value. And custom has it that they are included in the cost price in a murabahah sale, so it is permissible. But he does not include what he spent on himself during travel into the cost price, because merchants have not customarily included it in the cost price, nor...
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage expands on two rulings: the mudarib's expenses for short outings (dun al-safar) and the rules of murabahah (cost-plus sale) regarding inclusion of expenses. Regarding short outings: a distance less than three days and nights is considered travel if the mudarib goes out in the morning and does not return home for the night; in that case, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital because he is confined to the work. If he returns home for the night, it is like being in the city, and his expenses are from his own pocket. Regarding murabahah: when the mudarib sells the merchandise on a cost-plus basis, he may include all expenses directly on the merchandise (e.g., porterage, tailoring, brokerage, dyeing) in the cost price because they add value and custom so dictates. However, he may not include his own personal expenses during travel in the cost price, as merchants do not customarily do so. The passage also clarifies that if the mudarib spends from the capital, the owner first recovers the capital from the profit, then the remainder is profit to be divided. This ensures the capital is intact before profit is shared.

Key Terms
سفر (safar): Journey; defined as a distance of three days and nights in travel law.
مصر (misr): City or town; used here to distinguish between staying overnight in the city vs. traveling.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
If a mudarib goes to a nearby town (less than three days' journey) to conduct mudarabah business, leaves in the morning, and returns home at night, where do his personal expenses (like food) come from?
A) From the mudarabah capital, because the trip is for business
B) From his own pocket, because he returns home at night
C) Shared equally
D) From the profit only if profit is made

Key Takeaway
A mudarib's personal expenses are borne by the capital only when he stays overnight away from home for the sake of the business; otherwise, they are his own.

Inclusion of Expenses in Murabahah Sale
Ruling on which expenses are included in murabahah cost price

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to identify which expenses a mudarib may include in the cost price when selling merchandise on a murabahah basis, and explain why personal expenses are excluded.

Core Idea
Only expenses that directly increase the market value of the merchandise are includable in the murabahah cost price; personal travel expenses are not.

This lesson focuses on the second part of the passage, which addresses murabahah (cost‑plus sale). When the mudarib sells the merchandise on a cost‑plus basis, he may include expenses incurred directly on the goods, such as porterage and tailoring, because they add value and trade custom supports it. However, his own expenses during travel cannot be added. Understanding this distinction is crucial for proper pricing and trust in murabahah transactions.

Guided Path
1
Identify the murabahah transaction
The mudarib sells the merchandise on a cost‑plus basis, disclosing the cost price to the buyer.
2
List includable expenses
According to the passage, porterage, tailoring, brokerage, dyeing, and similar expenses directly on the merchandise are included in the cost price.
3
Understand the rationale
These expenses increase the value of the goods, and custom (urf) has established their inclusion in murabahah cost.
4
Exclude personal expenses
The mudarib's own travel expenses (food, lodging) are not included because trade custom does not treat them as part of cost.
5
Apply the murabahah rule
The seller says 'it cost me such‑and‑such,' including only direct merchandise costs; personal expenses are not added.

If he returns home for the night, it is like being in the city, and his expenses are from his own pocket. Regarding murabahah: when the mudarib sells the merchandise on a cost-plus basis, he may include all expenses directly on the merchandise (e.g., porterage, tailoring, brokerage, dyeing) in the cost price because they add value and custom so dictates. However, he may not include his own personal expenses during travel in the cost price, as merchants do not customarily do so. The passage also clarifies that if the mudarib spends from the capital, the owner first recovers the capital from the profit, then the remainder is profit to be divided. This ensures the capital is intact before profit is shared.

Key Terms
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit margin.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
Which of the following expenses may a mudarib include in the cost price when selling goods on a murabahah basis?
A) His own food during travel
B) Porterage fee for the goods
C) His own hotel bill
D) All of the above

Key Takeaway
In a murabahah, only expenses that directly add value to the merchandise and are customary may be included in the cost price; personal expenses of the mudarib are not.

Mudarib's Travel Expenses and Murabaha Cost Inclusion
Commentary on travel expenses and murabaha cost inclusion

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to distinguish between mudarib's travel expenses that are charged to the mudarabah capital versus those borne by the mudarib himself, and identify which costs are included in the cost basis for a murabaha sale.

Core Idea
A mudarib's expenses are on the capital only if he stays overnight away; only expenses enhancing the goods are counted in murabaha cost.

This lesson examines a key fiqh ruling on how a mudarib's expenses are classified in a mudarabah partnership. The commentary from 'Umdat al-Ri'ayah clarifies when travel expenses come from the capital and when they are personal. It also addresses which costs can be added to the cost price in a murabaha sale, relying on custom ('urf). Understanding these distinctions is crucial for correct profit calculation and avoiding disputes.

Guided Path
1
Identify the mudarib's travel patterns
Determine the two scenarios: (1) the mudarib goes out and does not spend the night at home; (2) he goes out but returns home to sleep.
The key criterion is whether he is absent from home overnight.
2
Apply the expense allocation rule
If the mudarib does not stay overnight, his expenses are from the mudarabah capital because he is confined for the partnership. If he returns home, he is like a local trader and bears his own expenses.
The ruling is based on the degree of connection to the partnership's work.
3
Differentiate between personal and business expenses in murabaha
When selling on markup, the mudarib may include expenses directly on the goods (carriage, brokerage, dyeing) because they increase the value. Personal expenses (food, lodging) are not included.
Custom ('urf) supports including only value-adding costs.
4
Understand the profit distribution sequence
If the mudarib spends from capital and later profits, the capital owner first recovers the full capital from the profit before dividing any surplus.
This ensures the capital is intact before profit sharing.
5
Recognize the role of custom
The inclusion of goods-related expenses in murabaha cost is validated by 'urf (common practice) among traders.
Custom is a recognized source in Hanafi fiqh for commercial transactions.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

[1] قوله: وما دون سفر...الخ؛ يعني إن كان خروج المضارب دون السفر، وهو مسيرة ثلاثة أيام ولياليها، فإن كان بحيث يغدو ويذهب إلى ما دونه، ولا يبيت بأهله؛ أي لا يكون في جميع الليل عند أهله، فهو في حكم السفر، فنفقته إن عمل فيه في مال المضاربة؛ لكون خروجه لأجلها، فصار محبوساً لها. وإن كان بحيث يغدو ثم يروح فيبيت بأهله، فحكمه حكم المصر، ويكون كالسوقي، فنفقته في مال نفسه؛ لأن أهل المصر يتجرون في المصر، ويبيتون في منازلهم. [2] قوله: أي أخذ الخ؛ حاصله: إن المضارب إذا أنفق من مال المضاربة فريحه يأخذه رب المال حتى يتم رأس ماله كاملاً، فيكون النفقة مصروفه إلى الربح دون رأس المال، فإذا استوفاه كان ما يبقى بينهما على ما شرط. [3] قوله: فإن فضل شيء قسم؛ فإن قسمة الربح إنما شرعت بعد تسليم رأس المال. (4) قوله: أي إن رابح ... الخ؛ يعني إن بايع المضارب مرابحة المتاع الذي أنفق عليه ضم جميع ما أنفق على المتاع من أجرة الحمال والطراز والسمسار والصباغ وغير ذلك مما تقدم في باب المرابحة إلى رأس المال، بأن يقول: قام علي هكذا؛ لأن هذه الأشياء تزيد في القيمة. والعرف جار بإلحاقها برأس المال في بيع المرابحة، فجاز ذلك، ولا يضم ما أنفقه على نفسه في سفر إلى رأس المال؛ لأن التجار لم يتعارفوا بضمه إلى رأس المال.
"And what is less than a journey that he goes to and does not spend the night with his family is like a journey, and if he spends the night, it is like the market of his city. Then if he profits, the owner of the capital takes from the profit what the mudarib spent from the capital. For if he sells on a profit basis, his expenses are accounted for the merchandise, not his own expenses." Commentary: That is, he takes from the profit what the mudarib spent from the capital until the capital is complete. Then if something remains, it is divided. "For if he sells on a profit basis, his expenses are accounted for the merchandise, not his own expenses": That is, if he sells on profit and says, 'It cost me such,' the expenses incurred on the merchandise, such as carriage hire and the like, are counted, and the mudarib's own expenses are not counted. [1] His saying: 'And what is less than a journey...' etc. means if the mudarib's departure is less than a journey, which is three days and their nights, and if he goes and returns during the day and does not spend the night with his family—i.e., he is not with them all night—then he is in the ruling of traveling, so his expenses are from the mudarabah capital if he works in it because his journey is for its sake, so he is confined for it. And if he goes and then returns and spends the night with his family, his ruling is the ruling of the city, and he is like a market-goer, so his expenses are from his own wealth because city dwellers trade in the city and sleep in their homes. [2] His saying: 'He takes...' etc.; the summary: If the mudarib spends from the mudarabah capital and then profits, the owner of the capital takes from the profit until his capital is complete, so the expenditure is charged to the profit, not to the capital. When he has recovered the capital, what remains is divided according to their agreement. [3] His saying: 'If something remains, it is divided': because the division of profit is only prescribed after the return of the capital. (4) His saying: 'For if he sells on a profit basis...' etc. means if the mudarib sells the merchandise on a markup basis, he includes in the cost all that he spent on the merchandise, such as porter's fee, tailoring, brokerage, dyeing, etc., as mentioned in the chapter on markup sale, by saying, 'It cost me such,' because these things increase the value. And custom is current in attaching them to the capital in markup sale, so that is permissible. And he does not include what he spent on himself while traveling to the capital because traders are not accustomed to including it.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage is a commentary (sharh) on a matn from Al-Wiqayah regarding the mudarib's expenses. The matn states the basic ruling: if the mudarib travels a distance less than a full journey (three days and nights) and does not stay overnight at home, he is considered a traveler and his expenses are borne by the mudarabah capital. However, if he goes out during the day but returns home at night, he is like a local trader and bears his own expenses. The key factor is whether the mudarib is absent from home overnight. The second part deals with how expenses are accounted for when selling goods on markup (murabaha). Expenses directly on the goods, such as carriage, brokerage, and dyeing, are added to the cost because they increase the value and custom supports their inclusion. The mudarib's personal expenses, such as food and accommodation while traveling, are not included because they do not enhance the goods' value and traders do not include them. The commentator also explains that if the mudarib spends from the capital and later profits, the owner first recovers the entire capital from the profit before dividing the remaining profit.

Key Terms
المضارب (al-mudarib): The working partner who contributes labor in a mudarabah partnership.
رب المال (rabb al-mal): The capital provider in a mudarabah partnership.
المرابحة (al-murabahah): A sale at a stated markup on the cost price, where the seller discloses the cost and adds a profit.
العرف (al-'urf): Custom or common practice, used as a secondary source of law.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) When he spends the night at home.
D) Always.

Key Takeaway
The mudarib's travel expenses are charged to capital only if he does not stay overnight at home; in murabaha, only expenses that enhance the goods' value are included in the cost, based on custom.

Mudarib's Profit and Loss: The Case of the Lost Price
Numerical example and commentary on mudarabah profit/loss sharing and murabaha cost disclosure.

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the distribution of profit and loss in a mudarabah transaction when the price is lost before payment, and determine the correct cost declaration for murabaha.

Core Idea
Profit is owned when it appears; subsequent loss is shared in proportion to ownership.

This passage presents a classic Hanafi case study that illustrates how profit and loss are allocated in a mudarabah when the mudarib realizes profit, reinvests, and then loses the price before payment. The example also clarifies how to declare cost in a subsequent murabaha sale. Understanding this case is essential for mastering the Hanafi rules of partnership and sale on markup.

Guided Path
1
Trace the initial transaction
Start from the beginning: the mudarib buys cloth for 1000 with the mudarabah capital, sells it for 2000. Calculate the profit (1000) and determine each party's share: mudarib gets half (500), owner gets half (500) as profit. At this point, the total fund is 2000, owned: mudarib 500 (his profit), owner 1500 (original 1000 + his profit 500).
2
Understand the reinvestment and ownership of the new asset
The mudarib uses all 2000 to buy a slave. Since the fund is owned proportionally, the slave is owned in the same ratio: mudarib owns 500/2000 = 1/4, owner owns 1500/2000 = 3/4.
Ownership is determined by the source of funds, not by who made the purchase.
3
Identify the loss event and its distribution
Before paying the seller, the 2000 is lost. The loss is borne according to ownership of the slave: mudarib loses 1/4 of 2000 = 500, owner loses 3/4 = 1500. Thus the mudarib's liability is 500, the owner's is 1500.
The loss is on the price, but it effectively destroys the asset's value proportionally.
4
Calculate the new capital after the owner covers the loss
To complete the transaction, the owner must pay the seller 2000. But the owner only lost 1500? Actually, the owner pays the full 2000? Wait: The owner had 1500 at risk, the mudarib 500. When the price is lost, the owner must still pay the seller? Actually, the text: 'when he pays it, the capital becomes 2500 because the owner first paid a thousand and then paid a thousand five hundred.' So the owner pays the seller 2000? Let's see: The owner initially contributed 1000. After loss, he must pay an additional 1500 to cover the mudarib's share? Actually, the text says: 'the owner first paid a thousand (original capital) and then paid a thousand five hundred' — that totals 2500. So the owner pays 1500 extra to cover the loss, making total capital 2500. The mudarib's liability of 500 is effectively a debt from the mudarib to the owner? But in the example, the mudarib does not pay; the owner pays the seller and the mudarib owes the owner 500? The text says 'gives' and 'capital becomes 2500'. So the new capital is 2500, but then for murabaha, the cost is declared as 2000. So the 500 loss is borne by the mudarib but not added to cost.
This step clarifies that the mudarib's loss is a separate debt, not part of the purchase cost.
5
Apply the murabaha cost disclosure rule
When the mudarib sells the slave on markup (murabaha), he must disclose the purchase cost. The cost is the price paid to the seller, which was 2000. The loss of 500 (wadi'ah) is not included because it is not part of the purchase price. Hence, he says 'it cost me 2000', not 2500.
The principle: losses from destruction are not included in cost for murabaha, whereas expenses that increase the asset's value are included.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مضارب بالنصف شرى بألفها بزاً، وباعه بألفين، وشرى بهما عبداً فضاعا في يده، غرم المضارب ربعهما، والمالك الباقي، وربع العبد للمضارب، وباقيه لها، ورأس المال ألفان وخمسمئة، ورابح على ألفين فقط. أي اشترى بالألف ثوباً، وباعه بألفين، وشرى بألفين عبداً، ولم يدفعهما إلى البائع، حتى ضاع الألفان في يد المضارب، غرم المضارب ربع الألفين؛ لأنَّه ملك المضارب والمالك ثلاثة الأرباع، فإذا دفعها يصير رأس المال ألفين وخمسمئة، لأنَّ رب المال دفع أولاً ألفاً، ثم دفع ألفاً وخمسمئة، فإن باعه مرابحة، يقول قام علي بألفين. وقوله: فقط؛ أي لا يقول: قام علي بألفين وخمسمئة؛ لأنَّ الشراء وقع بألفين، فلا يضم الوضيعة التي وقعت بسبب الهلاك في يد المضارب. يزيد هو أيضاً في قيمة المتاع، بخلاف الإنفاق على المتاع؛ لأنها بالزيادة على الثمن صارت في معنى الثمن. [1] قوله: بزاً؛ قال محمد في «السير الكبير»: البر عند أهل الكوفة: ثياب الكتان والقطن، لا ثياب الصوف والخزّ. كذا في «البناية»؛ لأنَّ المضارب لما باع البر بألفين ظهر الربح بقدر الألف، فيملك المضارب نصفه، وهو خمسمئة. فإذا اشترى بالألفين عبداً صار ربع العبد له، وثلاثة أرباعه لرب المال، فإذا هلك الثمن كان ما يخص الربع على المضارب، وما يخص ثلاثة أرباع على رب المال، و ظاهر على من له ذوق سليم، وطبع مستقيم. وهو [2] قوله: لأنه ملك المضارب ... الخ؛ تقريره: إنَّ المال لما صار ألفين ظهر الربح في المال وهو ألف، وكان بينهما نصفين، فنصيب المضارب منه خمسمئة، فإذا اشترى بالألفين عبداً صار مشتركاً بينهما، فربعُهُ للمضارب وثلاثة أرباعه لرب المال، ثم إذا ضاع الألفان قبل النقد كان عليهما ضمانُ العبد على قدر ملكهما في العبد، فربعه على
"A mudarib with half (profit share) bought cloth for a thousand, sold it for two thousand, and bought with the two thousand a slave, and the money was lost in his hand. The mudarib is liable for one quarter of the loss, and the owner for the rest. One quarter of the slave belongs to the mudarib, and the rest to her (the owner). The capital is two thousand five hundred, and the profit is on two thousand only." That is, he bought a garment for a thousand, sold it for two thousand, and bought a slave for two thousand without paying the seller until the two thousand was lost in the mudarib's hand. The mudarib is liable for one quarter of the two thousand because that proportion belongs to the mudarib, and the owner for three quarters. When he pays it, the capital becomes two thousand five hundred because the owner first paid a thousand and then paid a thousand five hundred. If he sells it on markup, he says, 'It cost me two thousand.' His statement 'only' means he does not say 'It cost me two thousand five hundred' because the purchase was for two thousand, and he does not include the loss that occurred due to destruction in the mudarib's hand. [That loss] also decreases the value of the merchandise, unlike expenditure on the merchandise because it increases the price and thereby becomes part of the price. [1] His saying: 'buzz' (cloth); Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'Al-burr (cloth) in the view of the people of Kufa is linen and cotton garments, not wool or silk.' Thus in al-Binayah. Because when the mudarib sold the cloth for two thousand, a profit of one thousand appeared; he owns half of it, which is five hundred. So when he bought a slave for two thousand, one quarter of the slave belongs to him and three quarters to the owner. When the price was lost, each bears the loss of the slave according to his ownership, so one quarter is on the mudarib and three quarters on the owner. And this is apparent to one of sound taste and upright nature. [2] His saying: 'because the mudarib owns...' etc.; its explanation: When the money became two thousand, the profit in the money became one thousand, and it was between them half each, so the mudarib's share is five hundred. Then when he bought a slave for two thousand, it became shared between them: one quarter for the mudarib, three quarters for the owner. Then when the two thousand was lost before payment, they are both liable for the slave according to their ownership: one quarter on the mudarib and three quarters on the owner.
Source pages p. 390

Commentary
This passage provides a detailed numerical example illustrating principles of mudarabah when profit appears and then a loss occurs before payment. The case: a mudarib entitled to half the profit buys cloth for 1000, sells for 2000, realizing a profit of 1000. He then uses the entire 2000 to buy a slave, but before paying the seller, the 2000 is lost. The loss is distributed according to ownership: the mudarib had a 500 share (his half of the profit), so he owns one quarter of the 2000, and the owner owns three quarters. Thus, the mudarib bears one quarter of the loss (500), and the owner bears three quarters (1500). After the owner pays the seller, the total capital becomes 2500 (original 1000 plus 1500 replacement). If the mudarib later sells the slave on murabaha, he declares the cost as 2000, not 2500, because the additional 500 was a loss borne by the mudarib, not a cost of purchase. The commentator also defines 'buzz' as linen/cotton garments per Kufan usage, quoting Imam Muhammad. The key principle: profit is owned proportionally when it appears, and subsequent loss is shared according to ownership. Only actual purchase cost, not subsequent losses, is declared in murabaha.

Key Terms
بز (buzz): A type of cloth; according to Kufan usage, linen and cotton garments.
الوضيعة (al-wadi'ah): Loss or deficiency, specifically the loss incurred by the mudarib from the destruction of the price.
مرابحة (murabahah): A sale at a markup on the disclosed cost.

Key Terms from Source Passage

C) 2500
D) 500

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudarabah, profit vests immediately, making the mudarib a co-owner; subsequent loss is shared proportionally, and only the purchase price is declared in murabaha, not subsequent losses.

Mudarib's Liability for Loss After Profit Realization
Explains mudarabah loss distribution when capital is lost after profit realization

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the distribution of loss between the mudarib and rabb al-mal when capital is lost after a profitable resale but before payment for a new purchase.

Core Idea
In mudarabah, a mudarib's profit share becomes his personal property and he bears its loss upon reinvestment.

This passage from the Hidayah commentary presents a concrete scenario to illustrate a critical Hanafi rule: when the mudarib realizes a profit from an initial transaction and then reinvests the entire amount, the profit share that belongs to him becomes his personal liability if the reinvested capital is lost. Understanding this allocation requires careful tracing of ownership and the nature of mudarabah property as a trust.

Guided Path
1
Trace the Transaction Sequence
List each step: initial capital (1000), purchase of cloth, sale for 2000 (profit 1000), reinvestment of the 2000 to buy a slave, loss of the 2000 before paying the seller.
The mudarib's profit share is half of the profit = 500.
2
Determine Ownership of the Reinvested Asset
The slave is purchased with 2000. The mudarib's share of the 2000 is his profit of 500, so he owns 1/4 of the slave; the rabb al-mal owns 3/4.
Ownership is proportional to contribution.
3
Apply Loss Allocation
When the 2000 cash is lost, liability falls according to ownership of the slave: the mudarib bears 1/4 of the loss (500), the rabb al-mal bears 3/4 (1500).
The mudarib's share is his personal property, not trust property, so it leaves the mudarabah.
4
Recalculate Capital Basis
After the loss compensation, the rabb al-mal has effectively paid an additional 1500, making total capital 2500. However, for future profit calculations (e.g., if sold on murābaḥah), the cost basis is only 2000, because the loss is not added to the purchase price.
The loss is a separate expense, not part of the acquisition cost.
5
Understand the Underlying Principle
Mudarabah property is a trust (amānah). Once the mudarib's profit share is determined, it becomes his property and cannot remain as trust. Thus, when reinvested, it is subject to liability if lost.
This prevents mixing of liability with trust status.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books
مضارب بالنِّصْفِ شَرَى بألفها بُزّاً، وباعه بألفين، وشَرَى بهما عبداً فضاعا في يده، غرم المضارب ربعهما، والمالك الباقي، وربع العبد للمضارب، وباقيه لها، ورأس المال ألفانِ وخمسمئة، ورابح على ألفين فقط) : أي اشترى بالألف ثوباً، وباعه بألفين، وشرى بألفين عبداً ، ولم يدفعهما إلى البائع، حتى ضاع الألفان في يد المضارب، غرمَ المضاربُ ربعَ الألفين؛ لأنَّه ملك المضارب (۳) والمالك ثلاثة الأرباع، فإذا دفعها يصير رأس المال ألفين وخمسمئة، لأنَّ ربِّ المالِ دَفَعَ أولاً ألفاً، ثُمَّ دَفَعَ ألفاً وخمسمئة، فإن باعَهُ مرابحة، يقولُ قام علي بألفين. وقوله : فقط؛ أي لا يقولُ: قامَ علي بألفين وخمسمئة؛ لأنَّ الشَّراءَ وَقَعَ بألفين، فلا يضمُّ الوضيعة التي وَقَعَتْ بسبب الهلاكِ في يد المضارب. يزيد هو أيضاً في قيمة المتاع، بخلاف الإنفاق على المتاع؛ لأنها بالزيادة على الثمن صارت في معنى الثمن. [1] قوله : بزاً؛ قال محمد في «السير الكبير»: البر عند أهل الكوفة : ثياب الكتانِ والقطن، لا ثياب الصوف والخزّ. كذا في «البناية» (۱)؛ لأنَّ المضارب لما باع البر بألفين ظهر الربح بقدر الألف، فيملك المضارب نصفه، وهو خمسمئة. فإذا اشترى بالألفين عبداً صار ربع العبد له، وثلاثة أرباعه لرب المال، فإذا هلك الثمن كان ما يخص الربع على المضارب، وما يخص ثلاثة أرباع على ربِّ المال، و ظاهر على من له ذوق سليم، وطبع مستقيم. وهو [٢] قوله : لأنه ملك المضارب ... الخ؛ تقريره : إنَّ المال لما صار ألفين ظهر الربح في المال وهو ألف، وكان بينهما نصفين، فنصيب المضارب منه خمسمئة، فإذا اشترى بالألفين عبداً صار مشتركاً بينهما، فربعُهُ للمضارب وثلاثة أرباعه لرب المال، ثم إذا ضاع الألفان قبل النقد كان عليهما ضمانُ العبد على قدر ملكهما في العبد، فربعه على المضارب، وهو خمسمئة وثلاثة أرباعه على المالك، وهو ألف وخمسمئة، ونصيب المضارب وهو الربع، يخرج من المضاربة؛ لأن نصيبه مضمون عليه، ومال المضاربة أمانة. ومن الظاهر أن بين كون الشيء وبين كونه أمانة منافاة؛ لأنه لو لم يخرج نصيبُ المضارب من المضاربة يجتمع الضمان مع كونه أمانة، وهذا غير جائز، ويبقى ثلاثة أرباع العبد على المضاربة؛ لأنه ليس فيه ما ينافي المضاربة؛ لأنّ رب المال موكل، وفرار الضمان إنما يكون على الموكل.
A mudarib [with share of] half: he bought with his thousand a garment, sold it for two thousand, and bought with them a slave, and the two thousand were lost in his hand. The mudarib pays a quarter of them, and the owner the rest, and a quarter of the slave belongs to the mudarib and the rest to the owner. The capital is two thousand five hundred, and profit only on two thousand. Explanation: He bought with a thousand a cloth, sold it for two thousand, and bought with two thousand a slave but did not pay the seller until the two thousand were lost in the mudarib's hand. The mudarib pays a quarter of the two thousand because it is the mudarib's ownership, and the owner three quarters. When he pays it, the capital becomes two thousand five hundred because the owner first paid a thousand, then paid a thousand five hundred. If he sells on credit saying 'cost me two thousand,' his statement 'only' means he does not say 'cost me two thousand five hundred,' because the purchase occurred for two thousand, so he does not include the loss that occurred due to destruction in the mudarib's hand. The same applies to increase in the value of the commodity, unlike expenditure on the commodity, because it [expenditure] by the increase on the price becomes in the meaning of the price. [1] His statement: 'bazz'; Muhammad said in al-Siyar al-Kabir: 'From the people of Kufa, cloth is linen and cotton garments, not wool and silk garments.' Likewise in al-Binaya. Because when the mudarib sold the cloth for two thousand, profit appeared to the extent of a thousand, so the mudarib owns half, which is five hundred. When he bought with two thousand a slave, a quarter of the slave belongs to him and three quarters to the owner. If the price is lost, then what pertains to a quarter is on the mudarib and what pertains to three quarters is on the owner. [2] His statement: 'because it is the mudarib's ownership...'; its explanation: when the money became two thousand, profit in the money appeared as a thousand, shared between them in halves, so the mudarib's share is five hundred. When he bought with two thousand a slave, it becomes jointly owned: a quarter for the mudarib and three quarters for the owner. Then, if the two thousand are lost before payment, the liability for the slave falls on both according to their ownership in the slave. So a quarter is on the mudarib, i.e., five hundred, and three quarters on the owner, i.e., one thousand five hundred. The mudarib's share, the quarter, leaves the mudarabah because his share is guaranteed upon him, and the mudarabah property is a trust. It is obvious that there is incompatibility between something being a property and being a trust; if the mudarib's share had not left the mudarabah, liability would be combined with trust, which is impermissible. The remaining three quarters of the slave remain in the mudarabah because there is nothing in it contradictory to mudarabah, since the owner is the principal, and the escape from liability is only on the principal.
Source pages p. 391

Commentary
This passage is a detailed ruling from the Hanafi school on how to handle a mudarabah scenario where the mudarib makes a series of transactions and then loses the capital. The author explains that when the mudarib buys cloth for 1000, sells for 2000, and uses that 2000 to buy a slave, but the 2000 is lost before paying, the loss is split: the mudarib bears a quarter (his share of profit from the first sale) and the owner bears three quarters. The capital is recalculated as 2500 because the owner effectively paid 1000 initially and then 1500 more. The mudarib's basis for profit calculation is only 2000, not including the loss. The commentary further clarifies that the mudarib's quarter share of the slave is his personal property, so it must be removed from the mudarabah trust to avoid merger of liability and trust. The remaining three quarters remain in the mudarabah.

Key Terms
مضارب (mudārib): The managing partner in a mudarabah contract, responsible for investment.
رب المال (rabb al-māl): The capital provider in a mudarabah contract.
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale with disclosure of cost and profit margin.
وضيعة (waḍīʿah): A loss or discount, here referring to the lost capital.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Checkpoint
In the mudarabah scenario, what fraction of the lost 2000 does the mudarib have to pay?
A) One half
B) One quarter
C) Three quarters
D) The entire amount

Key Takeaway
In Hanafi mudarabah, once profit is realized, the mudarib's share becomes his personal property; if reinvested and lost, he bears that portion of the loss.

Mudarabah — Profit-Sharing
intermediate
6 min
Profit Distribution in Mudarabah with Personal Share
Calculates profit when the slave is sold for double

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the profit distribution in a mudarabah when part of the sold item belongs personally to the mudarib.

Core Idea
The mudarib's personal share is excluded from the mudarabah before calculating profit.

In a mudarabah, the mudarib may invest property that is partly his own and partly from the partnership. When such a joint property is sold, we must first separate the mudarib's personal portion before computing the mudarabah's profit. This lesson walks through the Hanafi calculation using the example of a slave sold for double its value.

Guided Path
1
Identify the total sale price
In the example, the slave is sold for 4000, which is double the value of the mudarabah's share (originally 2500 capital plus some profit).
The total includes both the mudarabah's three-quarters and the mudarib's personal one-quarter.
2
Separate the mudarib's personal share
The mudarib owned one-quarter of the slave personally. At sale, that quarter is worth 1000. This amount belongs entirely to the mudarib and is not part of the mudarabah.
This step is crucial: the entire sale price is not mudarabah property.
3
Determine the mudarabah's portion
Subtract the mudarib's personal share (1000) from the total (4000). The remaining 3000 is the mudarabah's share of the proceeds.
This 3000 represents the three-quarters of the slave that belonged to the mudarabah.
4
Deduct the capital invested
From the mudarabah's portion (3000), subtract the original capital of 2500. The remainder is the profit generated by the mudarabah.
Capital is always returned to the owner before profit is divided.
5
Distribute the profit according to agreement
The remaining 500 is profit. In this scenario, the agreement was for equal sharing, so 250 goes to the owner and 250 to the mudarib.
The mudarib receives profit from his mudarabah role in addition to his personal 1000.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

If it is sold at double of them, then its share is three thousand, and the profit from it is half a thousand between them. Explanation: If it is sold for four thousand, then three thousand is the share of the mudarabah, and the thousand is the specific property of the mudarib. Then, from the three thousand, the capital is paid, which is two thousand five hundred, so remaining profit is five hundred: half for the owner, half for the mudarib.
Source pages p. 392

Commentary
This passage extends the previous scenario: after the mudarib's quarter of the slave becomes his personal property, the remaining three quarters (worth 3000 when sold for 4000) constitute the mudarabah property. The capital of 2500 is deducted from this 3000, leaving 500 profit. This profit is split equally between the owner and mudarib per their original agreement. The mudarib's personal thousand is not part of the mudarabah.

Key Terms
حصة المضاربة (ḥiṣṣat al-muḍārabah): The portion of proceeds belonging to the mudarabah partnership.
ربح (ribḥ): Profit after deducting capital.
رأس المال (raʾs al-māl): The capital invested in the mudarabah, to be returned to the owner before profit distribution.

Key Terms from Source Passage

Key Takeaway
In a mudarabah, the mudarib's personal property must be excluded before computing profit to avoid mixing personal and partnership funds.

Mudarib's Cost Basis in Murabaha When Buying from the Capital Owner
Explains profit calculation when mudarib buys a slave from owner

Learning Objective
By the end of this lesson, you will be able to calculate the mudarib's cost basis in a murabaha sale when the mudarib buys from the capital owner, applying the Hanafi rule that adds half the owner's profit.

Core Idea
The mudarib's murabaha cost equals the price paid plus half the owner's profit from the original purchase.

This lesson examines a specific scenario in mudarabah where the mudarib purchases an asset from the capital owner and later sells it on murabaha. The Hanafi school adjusts the cost basis to reflect the owner's profit, ensuring the mudarib reports the correct cost. This prevents understating cost and overstating profit, maintaining transparency in murabaha contracts.

Guided Path
1
Identify the owner's original cost and the market value
Determine what the capital owner paid for the asset and its current market value. The difference is the owner's profit.
If the owner bought the asset at market price, no profit adjustment is needed.
2
Calculate the owner's profit
Subtract the owner's cost from the market value. This profit belongs to the owner, but half is imputed to the mudarib's cost basis.
The rule derives from the mudarib's right to half the profit in the mudarabah.
3
Compute the mudarib's murabaha cost basis
Add half of the owner's profit to the price paid by the mudarib to the owner. The formula: Cost = Price paid + (Owner's profit / 2).
This is the amount the mudarib must disclose in the murabaha sale.
4
Apply to scenario variants
Test the formula with different figures: if mudarabah capital is larger, or if owner's profit is in value only (not price), adjust accordingly.
The text gives examples where capital is 1000, profit 1000, asset worth 2000; also when capital is 2000, and when value exceeds price.

Source Text
⚠ Extracted via Advanced OCR ∼97% accuracy, minor errors may remain — always verify against original Arabic books

And if he bought from the capital owner a slave for a thousand, he bought it at half of it. [3] His statement: 'a slave' meaning a slave whose value is a thousand, so price and value are equal. We only say that because if there was a surplus, such as the capital owner bought a slave worth two thousand and sold it to the mudarib for two thousand after the mudarib worked in his thousand and made a thousand profit, then he sells it in murabaha at one thousand five hundred (the mudarib's share). As for if the mudarabah capital was two thousand, it is like the first issue. Likewise, if there is a surplus in the value of the sold item but not in the price, for example the slave is worth one thousand five hundred, and the capital owner bought it for a thousand and sold it to the mudarib for a thousand, then the mudarib sells it in murabaha at one thousand two hundred and fifty, and the reverse of this...

Commentary
This passage discusses the case where the mudarib buys a slave from the capital owner. If the slave's market value equals the price, the cost basis is straightforward. If the owner bought the slave cheaply and sold it at market, the mudarib's cost basis includes the owner's profit. For instance, if mudarabah capital was 1000 and the mudarib earned 1000 profit, then buys a slave worth 2000 from the owner for 2000, the mudarib's basis for murabaha is 1500 (his share is 1000 profit = becomes capital, plus half of the remaining? Actually the text says 'on one thousand five hundred, the mudarib's share'). The author also covers cases where the mudarabah capital is larger or the slave's value is unequal. The end is cut off.

Key Terms
رب المال (rabb al-māl): Capital owner, who provides the mudarabah capital and also sells the asset.
مضارب (muḍārib): The entrepreneur in a mudarabah contract; in this scenario, also the buyer of the asset.
مرابحة (murābaḥah): A sale where the seller discloses the cost and adds a known profit.

Key Terms from Source Passage
